Revolution">Romanian Revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Singing_Revolution" title="Singing Revolution">Singing Revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bolivarian_Revolution" title="Bolivarian Revolution">Bolivarian Revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Overthrow_of_Slobodan_Milo%C5%A1evi%C4%87" title="Overthrow of Slobodan Milo\xc5\xa1evi\xc4\x87">Bulldozer Revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Rose_Revolution" title="Rose Revolution">Rose Revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Orange_Revolution" title="Orange Revolution">Orange Revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tulip_Revolution" title="Tulip Revolution">Tulip Revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_Revolution_of_2010" title="Kyrgyz Revolution of 2010">Kyrgyz Revolution of 2010</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tunisian_Revolution" title="Tunisian Revolution">Tunisian Revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Revolution" title="Yemeni Revolution">Yemeni Revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Euromaidan" title="Euromaidan">Euromaidan</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg" class="image"><img alt="A coloured voting box.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Revolution_sidebar" title="Template:Revolution sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Revolution_sidebar" title="Template talk:Revolution sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Revolution_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>American Revolution</b> was a colonial revolt that took place between 1765 and 1783. The American Patriots in the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> won independence from Great Britain, becoming the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States of America</a>. They defeated the British in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> (1775\xe2\x80\x931783) in alliance with France and others.\n</p><p>Members of <a href="/wiki/Colonial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Colonial history of the United States">American colonial society</a> argued the position of "<a href="/wiki/No_taxation_without_representation" title="No taxation without representation">no taxation without representation</a>", starting with the <a href="/wiki/Stamp_Act_Congress" title="Stamp Act Congress">Stamp Act Congress</a> in 1765. They rejected the authority of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">the British Parliament</a> to tax them because they lacked members in that governing body. Protests steadily escalated to the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Massacre" title="Boston Massacre">Boston Massacre</a> in 1770 and the <a href="/wiki/Gaspee_Affair" title="Gaspee Affair">burning of the <i>Gaspee</i></a> in <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Rhode_Island_and_Providence_Plantations" title="Colony of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations">Rhode Island</a> in 1772, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Tea_Party" title="Boston Tea Party">Boston Tea Party</a> in December 1773, during which Patriots destroyed a consignment of taxed tea. The British responded by closing Boston Harbor, then followed with a <a href="/wiki/Intolerable_Acts" title="Intolerable Acts">series of legislative acts</a> which effectively rescinded <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Colony" title="Massachusetts Bay Colony">Massachusetts Bay Colony</a>\'s rights of self-government and caused the other colonies to rally behind Massachusetts. In late 1774, the Patriots set up <a href="/wiki/First_Continental_Congress" title="First Continental Congress">their own alternative government</a> to better coordinate their resistance efforts against Great Britain; other colonists preferred to remain aligned to the Crown and were known as <a href="/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution)" title="Loyalist (American Revolution)"><i>Loyalists</i></a> or <i>Tories</i>.\n</p><p>Tensions erupted into battle between Patriot militia and British regulars when the king\'s army attempted to capture and destroy Colonial military supplies at <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Lexington_and_Concord" title="Battles of Lexington and Concord">Lexington and Concord</a> on April 19, 1775. The conflict then developed into a global war, during which the Patriots (and later their French, Spanish, and Dutch allies) fought the British and Loyalists in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> (1775\xe2\x80\x9383). Each of the thirteen colonies formed a <a href="/wiki/Provincial_Congress" title="Provincial Congress">Provincial Congress</a> that assumed power from the old colonial governments and suppressed Loyalism, and from there they built a <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> under the leadership of General <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>. The Continental Congress determined <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George</a>\'s rule to be tyrannical and infringing the colonists\' <a href="/wiki/Rights_of_Englishmen" title="Rights of Englishmen">rights as Englishmen</a>, and they declared the colonies <a href="/wiki/Lee_Resolution" title="Lee Resolution">free and independent states</a> on July 2, 1776. The Patriot leadership professed the political philosophies of <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Liberalism in the United States">liberalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republicanism</a> to reject monarchy and aristocracy, and they proclaimed that all men are created equal.\n</p><p>The Continental Army <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Boston" title="Siege of Boston">forced the redcoats out of Boston</a> in March 1776, but that summer <a href="/wiki/New_York_and_New_Jersey_campaign" title="New York and New Jersey campaign">the British captured and held New York City</a> and its strategic harbor for the duration of the war. The Royal Navy blockaded ports and captured other cities for brief periods, but they failed to defeat Washington\'s forces. The Patriots unsuccessfully <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Quebec_(1775)" title="Invasion of Quebec (1775)">attempted to invade Canada during the winter of 1775\xe2\x80\x9376</a>, but successfully captured a British army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Saratoga" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Saratoga">Battle of Saratoga</a> in October 1777. France now entered the war as an ally of the United States with a large army and navy that threatened Britain itself. The war turned to the American South where the British under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cornwallis,_1st_Marquess_Cornwallis" title="Charles Cornwallis, 1st Marquess Cornwallis">Charles Cornwallis</a> captured an army at <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston, South Carolina</a> in early 1780 but failed to enlist enough volunteers from Loyalist civilians to take effective control of the territory. A combined American\xe2\x80\x93French force captured a second British army <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown" title="Siege of Yorktown">at Yorktown</a> in the fall of 1781, effectively ending the war. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris</a> was signed September 3, 1783, formally ending the conflict and confirming the new nation\'s complete separation from the British Empire. The United States took possession of nearly all the territory east of the Mississippi River and south of the Great Lakes, with the British retaining control of Canada and Spain taking Florida.\n</p><p>Among the significant results of the revolution was the creation of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>, establishing a relatively strong federal national government that included an <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">executive</a>, a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Supreme Court">national judiciary</a>, and a bicameral <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Congress of the United States">Congress</a> that represented states in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> and the population in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood,_1992_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood,_1992-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The Revolution also resulted in the migration of around 60,000 Loyalists to other British territories, especially British North America (Canada).\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" <i>Annual Register</i></span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-56"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_territorial_growth_1775.svg" class="image"><img alt="Eastern North America in 1775. The British Province of Quebec, the thirteen colonies on the Atlantic coast, and the Indian reserve as defined by the Royal Proclamation of 1763. The 1763 Proclamation line is the border between the red and the pink areas, while the orange area represents the Spanish claim." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Map_of_territorial_growth_1775.svg/350px-Map_of_territorial_growth_1775.svg.png" width="350" height="458" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Map_of_territorial_growth_1775.svg/525px-Map_of_territorial_growth_1775.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Map_of_territorial_growth_1775.svg/700px-Map_of_territorial_growth_1775.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="506" data-file-height="662" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_territorial_growth_1775.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eastern North America in 1775. The <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Quebec_(1763%E2%80%931791)" title="Province of Quebec (1763\xe2\x80\x931791)">British Province of Quebec</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> on the Atlantic coast, and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Reserve_(1763)" title="Indian Reserve (1763)">Indian Reserve</a> as defined by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Proclamation_of_1763" title="Royal Proclamation of 1763">Royal Proclamation of 1763</a>. The border between the red and pink areas represents the 1763 "Proclamation line", while the orange area represents the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish claim</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span id="1651.E2.80.931748:_Early_seeds"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1651\xe2\x80\x931748:_Early_seeds">1651\xe2\x80\x931748: Early seeds</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Navigation_Acts" title="Navigation Acts">Navigation Acts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_New_England" title="Dominion of New England">Dominion of New England</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_Philip%27s_War" title="King Philip&#39;s War">King Philip\'s War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Austrian_Succession" title="War of the Austrian Succession">War of the Austrian Succession</a></div>\n<p>As early as 1651, the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">English government</a> had sought to regulate trade in the <a href="/wiki/British_America" title="British America">American colonies</a>. On October 9, the <a href="/wiki/Navigation_Acts#Navigation_Act_1651" title="Navigation Acts">Navigation Acts</a> were passed pursuant to a <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">mercantilist</a> policy intended to ensure that trade enriched only Great Britain, and barring trade with foreign nations.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Purvis1997_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Purvis1997-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Some argue that the economic impact was minimal on the colonists,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> but the political friction which the acts triggered was more serious, as the merchants most directly affected were most politically active.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/King_Philip%27s_War" title="King Philip&#39;s War">King Philip\'s War</a> ended in 1678, and much of it was fought without significant assistance from England. This contributed to the development of a unique identity, separate from that of the <a href="/wiki/Britishness" title="Britishness">British people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1680s, <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">King Charles II</a> determined to bring the <a href="/wiki/New_England_colonies" class="mw-redirect" title="New England colonies">New England colonies</a> under a more centralized administration in order to regulate trade more effectively.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> His efforts were fiercely opposed by the colonists, resulting in the abrogation of their colonial charter by the Crown.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Charles\' successor <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James II</a> finalized these efforts in 1686, establishing the <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_New_England" title="Dominion of New England">Dominion of New England</a>. Dominion rule triggered bitter resentment throughout New England; the enforcement of the unpopular Navigation Acts and the curtailing of local democracy angered the colonists.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> New Englanders were encouraged, however, by a <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">change of government in England</a> that saw James II effectively <a href="/wiki/Abdicate" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdicate">abdicate</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/1689_Boston_revolt" title="1689 Boston revolt">populist uprising</a> overthrew Dominion rule on April 18, 1689.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Colonial governments reasserted their control in the wake of the revolt, and successive governments made no more attempts to restore the Dominion.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Subsequent English governments continued in their efforts to tax certain goods, passing acts regulating the trade of <a href="/wiki/Wool_Act_1699" title="Wool Act 1699">wool</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hat_Act" title="Hat Act">hats</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Molasses_Act" title="Molasses Act">molasses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The Molasses Act of 1733 in particular was egregious to the colonists, as a significant part of colonial trade relied on the product. The taxes severely damaged the New England economy, and the taxes were rarely paid, resulting in a surge of smuggling, bribery, and intimidation of customs officials.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Colonial wars fought in America were often the source of considerable tension. The British captured the fortress of <a href="/wiki/Louisbourg" title="Louisbourg">Louisbourg</a> during the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Austrian_Succession" title="War of the Austrian Succession">War of the Austrian Succession</a>, but then ceded it back to France in 1748. New England colonists resented their losses of lives, as well as the effort and expenditure involved in subduing the fortress, only to have it returned to their erstwhile enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="floatleft"><a href="/wiki/File:1768_Boundary_Line_Map_Treaty_of_Ft_Stanwix.jpg" class="image" title="Boundary Line Map of 1768 move the boundary West"><img alt="Boundary Line Map of 1768 move the boundary West" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/1768_Boundary_Line_Map_Treaty_of_Ft_Stanwix.jpg/300px-1768_Boundary_Line_Map_Treaty_of_Ft_Stanwix.jpg" width="300" height="241" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/1768_Boundary_Line_Map_Treaty_of_Ft_Stanwix.jpg/450px-1768_Boundary_Line_Map_Treaty_of_Ft_Stanwix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/61/1768_Boundary_Line_Map_Treaty_of_Ft_Stanwix.jpg 2x" data-file-width="552" data-file-height="443" /></a></div><p> Historians typically begin their histories of the American Revolution with the British coalition victory in the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years&#39; War">Seven Years\' War</a> in 1763. The North American theater of the Seven Years\' War is commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a> in the United States; it removed France as a major player in North American affairs and led to the territory of <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">New France</a> being ceded to Great Britain. <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Henry_Gipson" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawrence Henry Gipson">Lawrence Henry Gipson</a> writes: </p><blockquote><p>It may be said as truly that the American Revolution was an aftermath of the Anglo-French conflict in the New World carried on between 1754 and 1763.</p></blockquote><p><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Proclamation_of_1763" title="Royal Proclamation of 1763">Royal Proclamation of 1763</a> may also have<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="Who says this? (September 2018)">weasel&#160;words</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> played a role in the separation of the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> from England, as colonists wanted to continue migrating west to lands awarded by the Crown for their wartime service.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Proclamation, however, cut them off. The lands west of Quebec and west of a line running along the crest of the <a href="/wiki/Allegheny_Mountains" title="Allegheny Mountains">Allegheny Mountains</a> became Indian territory, barred to settlement for two years.\n</p><p>The colonists protested, and the boundary line was adjusted in a series of treaties with the Indians. In 1768, Indians agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Fort_Stanwix" title="Treaty of Fort Stanwix">Treaty of Fort Stanwix</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Hard_Labour" title="Treaty of Hard Labour">Treaty of Hard Labour</a>, followed in 1770 by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lochaber" title="Treaty of Lochaber">Treaty of Lochaber</a>. The treaties opened most of Kentucky and West Virginia to colonial settlement. The new map was drawn up at the Treaty of Fort Stanwix in 1768 which moved the line much farther to the west, from the green line to the red line on the map at right.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1764.E2.80.931766:_Taxes_imposed_and_withdrawn"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1764\xe2\x80\x931766:_Taxes_imposed_and_withdrawn">1764\xe2\x80\x931766: Taxes imposed and withdrawn</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sugar_Act" title="Sugar Act">Sugar Act</a>, <a href="/wiki/Currency_Act" title="Currency Act">Currency Act</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quartering_Acts" title="Quartering Acts">Quartering Acts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stamp_Act_1765" title="Stamp Act 1765">Stamp Act 1765</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Declaratory_Act" title="Declaratory Act">Declaratory Act</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/No_taxation_without_representation" title="No taxation without representation">No taxation without representation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virtual_representation" title="Virtual representation">Virtual representation</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Parliament_Stamp_Act1765.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Parliament_Stamp_Act1765.jpg/220px-Parliament_Stamp_Act1765.jpg" width="220" height="423" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Parliament_Stamp_Act1765.jpg/330px-Parliament_Stamp_Act1765.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/40/Parliament_Stamp_Act1765.jpg 2x" data-file-width="416" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Parliament_Stamp_Act1765.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Notice of Stamp Act of 1765 in newspaper</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1764, Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Currency_Act" title="Currency Act">Currency Act</a> to restrain the use of paper money, fearing that otherwise the colonists might evade debt payments.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Parliament also passed the <a href="/wiki/Sugar_Act" title="Sugar Act">Sugar Act</a>, imposing customs <a href="/wiki/Duty_(economics)" title="Duty (economics)">duties</a> on a number of articles. That same year, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/George_Grenville" title="George Grenville">George Grenville</a> proposed direct taxes on the colonies to raise revenue, but he delayed action to see whether the colonies would propose some way to raise the revenue themselves.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Parliament finally passed the Stamp Act in March 1765 which imposed direct taxes on the colonies for the first time. All official documents, newspapers, almanacs, and pamphlets were required to have the stamps\xe2\x80\x94even decks of playing cards.\n</p><p>The colonists did not object that the taxes were high; they were actually low.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> They objected to the fact that they had no representation in the Parliament, and thus no voice concerning legislation that affected them. <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> testified in Parliament in 1766 that Americans already contributed heavily to the defense of the Empire. He said that local governments had raised, outfitted, and paid 25,000 soldiers to fight France\xe2\x80\x94as many as Britain itself sent\xe2\x80\x94and spent many millions from American treasuries doing so in the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a> alone.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> London had to deal with 1,500 politically well-connected British Army soldiers. The decision was to keep them on active duty with full pay, but they had to be stationed somewhere. Stationing a <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a> in Great Britain during peacetime was politically unacceptable, so the decision was made to station them in America and have the Americans pay them. The soldiers had no military mission; they were not there to defend the colonies because there was no threat to the colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_Liberty" title="Sons of Liberty">Sons of Liberty</a> were formed in 1765. They used public demonstrations, <a href="/wiki/Boycott" title="Boycott">boycott</a>, violence, and threats of violence to ensure that the British tax laws were unenforceable. In Boston, the Sons of Liberty burned the records of the <a href="/wiki/Vice_admiralty_court" title="Vice admiralty court">vice admiralty court</a> and looted the home of chief justice <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hutchinson_(governor)" title="Thomas Hutchinson (governor)">Thomas Hutchinson</a>. Several legislatures called for united action, and nine colonies sent delegates to the <a href="/wiki/Stamp_Act_Congress" title="Stamp Act Congress">Stamp Act Congress</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> in October 1765. Moderates led by <a href="/wiki/John_Dickinson_(delegate)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Dickinson (delegate)">John Dickinson</a> drew up a "<a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Rights_and_Grievances" title="Declaration of Rights and Grievances">Declaration of Rights and Grievances</a>" stating that taxes passed without representation violated their <a href="/wiki/Rights_of_Englishmen" title="Rights of Englishmen">rights as Englishmen</a>. Colonists emphasized their determination by boycotting imports of British merchandise.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Parliament at Westminster saw itself as the supreme lawmaking authority throughout all British possessions and thus entitled to levy any tax without colonial approval.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> They argued that the colonies were legally British corporations that were completely subordinate to the British parliament and pointed to numerous instances where Parliament had made laws binding on the colonies in the past.<sup id="cite_ref-Lecky,_William_Edward_Hartpole_1882_pp._297\xe2\x80\x9398_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lecky,_William_Edward_Hartpole_1882_pp._297\xe2\x80\x9398-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> They did not see anything in the unwritten British constitution that made taxes special<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and noted that they had taxed American trade for decades. Parliament insisted that the colonies effectively enjoyed a "<a href="/wiki/Virtual_representation" title="Virtual representation">virtual representation</a>" as most British people did, as only a small minority of the British population elected representatives to Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Americans such as <a href="/wiki/James_Otis,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="James Otis, Jr.">James Otis</a> maintained that the Americans were not in fact virtually represented.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In London, the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Watson-Wentworth,_2nd_Marquess_of_Rockingham" title="Charles Watson-Wentworth, 2nd Marquess of Rockingham">Rockingham</a> government came to power (July 1765) and Parliament debated whether to repeal the stamp tax or to send an army to enforce it. Benjamin Franklin made the case for repeal, explaining that the colonies had spent heavily in manpower, money, and blood in defense of the empire in a series of wars against the French and Indians, and that further taxes to pay for those wars were unjust and might bring about a rebellion. Parliament agreed and repealed the tax (February 21, 1766), but insisted in the <a href="/wiki/Declaratory_Act" title="Declaratory Act">Declaratory Act</a> of March 1766 that they retained full power to make laws for the colonies "in all cases whatsoever".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The repeal nonetheless caused widespread celebrations in the colonies.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1767.E2.80.931773:_Townshend_Acts_and_the_Tea_Act"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1767\xe2\x80\x931773:_Townshend_Acts_and_the_Tea_Act">1767\xe2\x80\x931773: Townshend Acts and the Tea Act</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Townshend_Acts" title="Townshend Acts">Townshend Acts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tea_Act" title="Tea Act">Tea Act</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Circular_Letter" title="Massachusetts Circular Letter">Massachusetts Circular Letter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston_Massacre" title="Boston Massacre">Boston Massacre</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Boston_Tea_Party" title="Boston Tea Party">Boston Tea Party</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaspee_Affair.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Burning of the Gaspee" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Gaspee_Affair.jpg/220px-Gaspee_Affair.jpg" width="220" height="348" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Gaspee_Affair.jpg/330px-Gaspee_Affair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Gaspee_Affair.jpg/440px-Gaspee_Affair.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1504" data-file-height="2376" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaspee_Affair.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Burning of the <i><a href="/wiki/Gaspee_Affair" title="Gaspee Affair">Gaspee</a></i></center></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1767, the Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Townshend_Acts" title="Townshend Acts">Townshend Acts</a> which placed duties on a number of essential goods, including paper, glass, and tea, and established a Board of Customs in Boston to more rigorously execute trade regulations. The new taxes were enacted on the belief that Americans only objected to internal taxes and not to external taxes such as custom duties. The Americans, however, argued against the constitutionality of the act because its purpose was to raise revenue and not regulate trade.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Colonists responded by organizing new boycotts of British goods. These boycotts were less effective, however, as the Townshend goods were widely used.\n</p><p>In February 1768, the Assembly of Massachusetts Bay <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Circular_Letter" title="Massachusetts Circular Letter">issued a circular letter</a> to the other colonies urging them to coordinate resistance. The governor dissolved the assembly when it refused to rescind the letter. Meanwhile, a riot broke out in Boston in June 1768 over the seizure of the sloop <i>Liberty</i>, owned by <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock" title="John Hancock">John Hancock</a>, for alleged smuggling. Customs officials were forced to flee, prompting the British to deploy troops to Boston. A Boston town meeting declared that no obedience was due to parliamentary laws and called for the convening of a convention. A convention assembled but only issued a mild protest before dissolving itself. In January 1769, Parliament responded to the unrest by reactivating the <a href="/wiki/Treason_Act_1543" title="Treason Act 1543">Treason Act 1543</a> which called for subjects outside the realm to face trials for treason in England. The governor of Massachusetts was instructed to collect evidence of said treason, and the threat caused widespread outrage, though it was not carried out.\n</p><p>On March 5, 1770, a large crowd gathered around a group of British soldiers. The crowd grew threatening, throwing snowballs, rocks, and debris at them. One soldier was clubbed and fell.<sup id="cite_ref-Hiller_B._Zobel_1996_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hiller_B._Zobel_1996-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> There was no order to fire, but the soldiers fired into the crowd anyway. They hit 11 people; three civilians died at the scene of the shooting, and two died after the incident. The event quickly came to be called the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Massacre" title="Boston Massacre">Boston Massacre</a>. The soldiers were tried and acquitted (defended by <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>), but the widespread descriptions soon began to turn colonial sentiment against the British. This, in turn, began a downward spiral in the relationship between Britain and the Province of Massachusetts.<sup id="cite_ref-Hiller_B._Zobel_1996_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hiller_B._Zobel_1996-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A new ministry under <a href="/wiki/Lord_North" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord North">Lord North</a> came to power in 1770, and Parliament withdrew all taxes except the tax on tea, giving up its efforts to raise revenue while maintaining the right to tax. This temporarily resolved the crisis, and the boycott of British goods largely ceased, with only the more radical patriots such as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Adams" title="Samuel Adams">Samuel Adams</a> continuing to agitate.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boston_Tea_Party_Currier_colored.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Two ships in a harbor, one in the distance. On board, men stripped to the waist and wearing feathers in their hair are throwing crates into the water. A large crowd, mostly men, is standing on the dock, waving hats and cheering. A few people wave their hats from windows in a nearby building." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Boston_Tea_Party_Currier_colored.jpg/250px-Boston_Tea_Party_Currier_colored.jpg" width="250" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Boston_Tea_Party_Currier_colored.jpg/375px-Boston_Tea_Party_Currier_colored.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Boston_Tea_Party_Currier_colored.jpg/500px-Boston_Tea_Party_Currier_colored.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1128" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boston_Tea_Party_Currier_colored.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>This 1846 <a href="/wiki/Lithography" title="Lithography">lithograph</a> by <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Currier" title="Nathaniel Currier">Nathaniel Currier</a> was entitled <i>The Destruction of Tea at Boston Harbor</i>; the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Boston_Tea_Party" title="Boston Tea Party">Boston Tea Party</a>" had not yet become standard.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></center></div></div></div>\n<p>In June 1772, American <a href="/wiki/Patriot_(American_Revolution)" title="Patriot (American Revolution)">patriots</a>, including <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(Rhode_Island)" title="John Brown (Rhode Island)">John Brown</a>, burned a British warship that had been vigorously enforcing unpopular trade regulations in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Gaspee_Affair" title="Gaspee Affair"><i>Gaspee</i> Affair</a>. The affair was investigated for possible treason, but no action was taken.\n</p><p>In 1772, it became known that the Crown intended to pay fixed salaries to the governors and judges in Massachusetts. Samuel Adams in Boston set about creating new Committees of Correspondence, which linked Patriots in all 13 colonies and eventually provided the framework for a rebel government. Virginia, the largest colony, set up its Committee of Correspondence in early 1773, on which Patrick Henry and Thomas Jefferson served.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A total of about 7000 to 8000 Patriots served on "Committees of Correspondence" at the colonial and local levels, comprising most of the leadership in their communities. Loyalists were excluded. The committees became the <a href="/wiki/Leadership" title="Leadership">leaders</a> of the American resistance to British actions, and largely determined the war effort at the state and local level. When the First Continental Congress decided to boycott British products, the colonial and local Committees took charge, examining merchant records and publishing the names of merchants who attempted to defy the boycott by importing British goods.<sup id="cite_ref-Mary_Beth_Norton_2001_pp_144_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mary_Beth_Norton_2001_pp_144-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1773, <a href="/wiki/Hutchinson_Letters_Affair" title="Hutchinson Letters Affair">private letters were published</a> in which Massachusetts Governor Thomas Hutchinson claimed that the colonists could not enjoy all English liberties, and Lieutenant Governor <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Oliver" title="Andrew Oliver">Andrew Oliver</a> called for the direct payment of colonial officials. The letters\' contents were used as evidence of a systematic plot against American rights, and discredited Hutchinson in the eyes of the people; the Assembly petitioned for his recall. <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>, postmaster general for the colonies, acknowledged that he leaked the letters, which led to him being berated by British officials and fired from his job.\n</p><p>Meanwhile, Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Tea_Act" title="Tea Act">Tea Act</a> to lower the price of taxed tea exported to the colonies in order to help the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> undersell smuggled Dutch tea. Special consignees were appointed to sell the tea in order to bypass colonial merchants. The act was opposed by those who resisted the taxes and also by smugglers who stood to lose business.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In most instances, the consignees were forced to resign and the tea was turned back, but Massachusetts governor Hutchinson refused to allow Boston merchants to give in to pressure. A town meeting in Boston determined that the tea would not be landed, and ignored a demand from the governor to disperse. On December 16, 1773, a group of men, led by Samuel Adams and dressed to evoke the appearance of American Indians, boarded the ships of the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">British East India Company</a> and dumped \xc2\xa310,000 worth of tea from their holds (approximately \xc2\xa3636,000 in 2008) into Boston Harbor. Decades later, this event became known as the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Tea_Party" title="Boston Tea Party">Boston Tea Party</a> and remains a significant part of American patriotic lore.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1774.E2.80.931775:_Intolerable_Acts_and_the_Quebec_Act"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1774\xe2\x80\x931775:_Intolerable_Acts_and_the_Quebec_Act">1774\xe2\x80\x931775: Intolerable Acts and the Quebec Act</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RapeBoston.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A 1774 etching from The London Magazine, copied by Paul Revere of Boston. Prime Minister Lord North, author of the Boston Port Act, forces the Intolerable Acts down the throat of America, whose arms are restrained by Lord Chief Justice Mansfield, while the 4th Earl of Sandwich pins down her feet and peers up her skirt. Behind them, Mother Britannia weeps helplessly." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/RapeBoston.jpg/250px-RapeBoston.jpg" width="250" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/RapeBoston.jpg/375px-RapeBoston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/RapeBoston.jpg/500px-RapeBoston.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1450" data-file-height="1058" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RapeBoston.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>A 1774 etching from <i><a href="/wiki/The_London_Magazine" title="The London Magazine">The London Magazine</a></i>, copied by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Revere" title="Paul Revere">Paul Revere</a> of <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frederick_North,_Lord_North" title="Frederick North, Lord North">Prime Minister Lord North</a>, author of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Port_Act" title="Boston Port Act">Boston Port Act</a>, forces the <a href="/wiki/Intolerable_Acts" title="Intolerable Acts">Intolerable Acts</a> down the throat of America, whose arms are restrained by <a href="/wiki/William_Murray,_1st_Earl_of_Mansfield" title="William Murray, 1st Earl of Mansfield">Lord Chief Justice Mansfield</a>, while <a href="/wiki/John_Montagu,_4th_Earl_of_Sandwich" title="John Montagu, 4th Earl of Sandwich">Lord Sandwich</a> pins down her feet and peers up her robes. Behind them, <a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">Mother Britannia</a> weeps helplessly.</center></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Act" title="Quebec Act">Quebec Act</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intolerable_Acts" title="Intolerable Acts">Intolerable Acts</a></div>\n<p>The British government responded by passing several Acts which came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Intolerable_Acts" title="Intolerable Acts">Intolerable Acts</a>, which further darkened colonial opinion towards the British. They consisted of four laws enacted by the British parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The first was the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Government_Act" title="Massachusetts Government Act">Massachusetts Government Act</a> which altered the Massachusetts charter and restricted town meetings. The second act was the <a href="/wiki/Administration_of_Justice_Act_1774" title="Administration of Justice Act 1774">Administration of Justice Act</a> which ordered that all British soldiers to be tried were to be arraigned in Britain, not in the colonies. The third Act was the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Port_Act" title="Boston Port Act">Boston Port Act</a>, which closed the port of Boston until the British had been compensated for the tea lost in the Boston Tea Party. The fourth Act was the <a href="/wiki/Quartering_Acts" title="Quartering Acts">Quartering Act of 1774</a>, which allowed royal governors to house British troops in the homes of citizens without requiring permission of the owner.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In response, Massachusetts patriots issued the <a href="/wiki/Suffolk_Resolves" title="Suffolk Resolves">Suffolk Resolves</a> and formed an alternative shadow government known as the "Provincial Congress" which began training militia outside British-occupied Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In September 1774, the <a href="/wiki/First_Continental_Congress" title="First Continental Congress">First Continental Congress</a> convened, consisting of representatives from each of the colonies, to serve as a vehicle for deliberation and collective action. During secret debates, conservative <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Galloway" title="Joseph Galloway">Joseph Galloway</a> proposed the creation of a colonial Parliament that would be able to approve or disapprove of acts of the British Parliament, but his idea was not accepted. The Congress instead endorsed the proposal of John Adams that Americans would obey Parliament voluntarily but would resist all taxes in disguise. Congress called for a boycott beginning on 1 December 1774 of all British goods; it was enforced by new committees authorized by the Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_hostilities_begin">Military hostilities begin</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Join, or Die by Benjamin Franklin was recycled to encourage the former colonies to unite against British rule" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg/250px-Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg" width="250" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg/375px-Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg/500px-Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3740" data-file-height="2696" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center><i><a href="/wiki/Join,_or_Die" title="Join, or Die">Join, or Die</a></i> by Benjamin Franklin was recycled to encourage the former colonies to unite against British rule.</center></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Shot_heard_%27round_the_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Shot heard &#39;round the world">Shot heard \'round the world</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston_campaign" title="Boston campaign">Boston campaign</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Canada_(1775)" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Canada (1775)">Invasion of Canada (1775)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<p>Massachusetts was declared in a state of rebellion in February 1775 and the British garrison received orders to disarm the rebels and arrest their leaders, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Lexington_and_Concord" title="Battles of Lexington and Concord">Battles of Lexington and Concord</a> on 19 April 1775. The Patriots laid siege to Boston, expelled royal officials from all the colonies, and took control through the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Provincial_Congress" title="Provincial Congress">Provincial Congresses</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bunker_Hill" title="Battle of Bunker Hill">Battle of Bunker Hill</a> followed on June 17, 1775. It was a British victory\xe2\x80\x94but at a great cost: about 1,000 British casualties from a garrison of about 6,000, as compared to 500 American casualties from a much larger force.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The Second Continental Congress was divided on the best course of action, but eventually produced the <a href="/wiki/Olive_Branch_Petition" title="Olive Branch Petition">Olive Branch Petition</a>, in which they attempted to come to an accord with <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George</a>. The king, however, issued a <a href="/wiki/Proclamation_of_Rebellion" title="Proclamation of Rebellion">Proclamation of Rebellion</a> which stated that the states were "in rebellion" and the members of Congress were traitors.\n</p><p>In the winter of 1775, the Americans <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Canada_(1775)" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Canada (1775)">invaded Canada</a> under generals <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Arnold" title="Benedict Arnold">Benedict Arnold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Montgomery" title="Richard Montgomery">Richard Montgomery</a>. The attack was a complete failure; many Americans who weren\'t killed were either captured or died of smallpox.\n</p><p>In March 1776, the Continental Army forced the British to <a href="/wiki/Evacuation_Day_(Massachusetts)" title="Evacuation Day (Massachusetts)">evacuate Boston</a>, with George Washington as the commander of the new army. The revolutionaries were now in full control of all 13 colonies and were ready to declare independence. There still were many Loyalists, but they were no longer in control anywhere by July 1776, and all of the Royal officials had fled.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Creating_new_state_constitutions">Creating new state constitutions</span></h2>\n<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bunker_Hill" title="Battle of Bunker Hill">Battle of Bunker Hill</a> in June 1775, the Patriots had control of Massachusetts outside the Boston city limits, and the Loyalists suddenly found themselves on the defensive with no protection from the British army. In all 13 colonies, Patriots had overthrown their existing governments, closing courts and driving away British officials. They had elected conventions and "legislatures" that existed outside any legal framework; new constitutions were drawn up in each state to supersede royal charters. They declared that they were states now, not colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-Pole-ch29_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pole-ch29-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On January 5, 1776, <a href="/wiki/Province_of_New_Hampshire" title="Province of New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> ratified the first state constitution. In May 1776, Congress voted to suppress all forms of crown authority, to be replaced by locally created authority. Virginia, <a href="/wiki/Province_of_South_Carolina" title="Province of South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, and New Jersey created their constitutions before July 4. <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Rhode_Island_and_Providence_Plantations" title="Colony of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations">Rhode Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Colony" title="Connecticut Colony">Connecticut</a> simply took their existing <a href="/wiki/Royal_charter" title="Royal charter">royal charters</a> and deleted all references to the crown.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The new states were all committed to republicanism, with no inherited offices. They decided what form of government to create, and also how to select those who would craft the constitutions and how the resulting document would be ratified. On 26 May 1776, <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> wrote James Sullivan from Philadelphia:\n</p><p>"Depend upon it, sir, it is dangerous to open so fruitful a source of controversy and altercation, as would be opened by attempting to alter the qualifications of voters. There will be no end of it. New claims will arise. Women will demand a vote. Lads from twelve to twenty one will think their rights not enough attended to, and every man, who has not a farthing, will demand an equal voice with any other in all acts of state. It tends to confound and destroy all distinctions, and prostrate all ranks, to one common level".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The resulting constitutions in states such as <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Maryland" title="Province of Maryland">Maryland</a>, Virginia, <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Colony" title="Delaware Colony">Delaware</a>, New York, and <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Massachusetts" title="Constitution of Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> featured:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Property qualifications for voting and even more substantial requirements for elected positions (though New York and Maryland lowered property qualifications)<sup id="cite_ref-Pole-ch29_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pole-ch29-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bicameral_legislature" class="mw-redirect" title="Bicameral legislature">Bicameral legislatures</a>, with the upper house as a check on the lower</li>\n<li>Strong <a href="/wiki/Governor" title="Governor">governors</a> with veto power over the legislature and substantial appointment authority</li>\n<li>Few or no restraints on individuals holding multiple positions in government</li>\n<li>The continuation of <a href="/wiki/Established_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Established church">state-established religion</a></li></ul>\n<p>In Pennsylvania, New Jersey, and New Hampshire, the resulting constitutions embodied:\n</p>\n<ul><li>universal manhood suffrage, or minimal property requirements for voting or holding office (New Jersey enfranchised some property-owning widows, a step that it retracted 25 years later)</li>\n<li>strong, <a href="/wiki/Unicameralism" title="Unicameralism">unicameral legislatures</a></li>\n<li>relatively weak governors without veto powers, and with little appointing authority</li>\n<li>prohibition against individuals holding multiple government posts</li></ul>\n<p>The radical provisions of Pennsylvania\'s constitution lasted only 14 years. In 1790, conservatives gained power in the state legislature, called a new constitutional convention, and rewrote the constitution. The new constitution substantially reduced universal male suffrage, gave the governor veto power and patronage appointment authority, and added an upper house with substantial wealth qualifications to the unicameral legislature. Thomas Paine called it a constitution unworthy of America.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood,_1992_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood,_1992-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_and_Union">Independence and Union</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III,_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg/250px-Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg" width="250" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg/375px-Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg/500px-Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1597" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III,_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel" title="Johannes Adam Simon Oertel">Johannes Adam Simon Oertel</a>. <i>Pulling Down the Statue of King George III, N.Y.C.</i>, ca. 1859</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lee_Resolution" title="Lee Resolution">Lee Resolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Five" title="Committee of Five">Committee of Five</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a></div>\n<p>In April 1776, the <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Provincial_Congress" title="North Carolina Provincial Congress">North Carolina Provincial Congress</a> issued the <a href="/wiki/Halifax_Resolves" title="Halifax Resolves">Halifax Resolves</a> explicitly authorizing its delegates to vote for independence.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In May, Congress called on all the states to write constitutions and eliminate the last remnants of royal rule.\n</p><p>By June, nine colonies were ready for independence; one by one, the last four fell into line: Pennsylvania, Delaware, Maryland, and New York. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Henry_Lee" title="Richard Henry Lee">Richard Henry Lee</a> was instructed by the Virginia legislature to propose independence, and he did so on June 7, 1776. On June 11, a committee was created to draft a document explaining the justifications for separation from Britain. After securing enough votes for passage, independence was voted for on July 2. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> was drafted largely by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> and presented by the committee; it was unanimously adopted by the entire Congress on July 4,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and each of the colonies became independent and sovereign. The next step was to form a union to facilitate international relations and alliances.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Founding Fathers represented a cross-section of Patriot  leadership. According to a study of the biographies of the 56 men who signed the Declaration of Independence: \n</p>\n<dl><dd>The  Signers came for the most part from an educated elite, were residents of  older settlements, and belonged with a few exceptions to a moderately  well-to-do class representing only a fraction of the population. Native  or born overseas, they were of British stock and of the Protestant faith.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>The Second Continental Congress approved the "Articles of Confederation" for ratification by the states on November 15, 1777; the Congress immediately began operating under the Articles\' terms, providing a structure of shared sovereignty during prosecution of the war and facilitating international relations and alliances with France and Spain. The articles were ratified on March 1, 1781.  At that point, the Continental Congress was dissolved and a new government of the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">United States in Congress Assembled</a> took its place on the following day, with <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Huntington_(statesman)" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel Huntington (statesman)">Samuel Huntington</a> as presiding officer.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Defending_the_Revolution">Defending the Revolution</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<h3><span id="British_return:_1776.E2.80.931777"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="British_return:_1776\xe2\x80\x931777">British return: 1776\xe2\x80\x931777</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/New_York_and_New_Jersey_campaign" title="New York and New Jersey campaign">New York and New Jersey campaign</a>, <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island_Peace_Conference" title="Staten Island Peace Conference">Staten Island Peace Conference</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saratoga_campaign" title="Saratoga campaign">Saratoga campaign</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_campaign" title="Philadelphia campaign">Philadelphia campaign</a></div>\n<p>According to British historian <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Black_(historian)" title="Jeremy Black (historian)">Jeremy Black</a>, the British had significant advantages, including a highly trained army, the world\'s largest navy, and a highly efficient system of public finance that could easily fund the war. However, the British were seriously handicapped by their misunderstanding of the depth of support for the Patriot position. Ignoring the advice of General Gage, they misinterpreted the situation as merely a large-scale riot. London decided that they could overawe the Americans by sending a large military and naval force, forcing them to be loyal again:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Convinced that the Revolution was the work of a full few miscreants who had rallied an armed rabble to their cause, they expected that the revolutionaries would be intimidated\xe2\x80\xa6. Then the vast majority of Americans, who were loyal but cowed by the terroristic tactics\xe2\x80\xa6 would rise up, kick out the rebels, and restore loyal government in each colony.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Washington forced the British out of Boston in the spring of 1776, and neither the British nor the Loyalists controlled any significant areas. The British, however, were massing forces at their naval base at <a href="/wiki/Halifax_Regional_Municipality" class="mw-redirect" title="Halifax Regional Municipality">Halifax, Nova Scotia</a>. They returned in force in July 1776, landing in New York and defeating Washington\'s Continental Army in August at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Long_Island" title="Battle of Long Island">Battle of Brooklyn</a>. Following that victory, the British requested a meeting with representatives from Congress to negotiate an end to hostilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Schecter,_Barnet_2002_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schecter,_Barnet_2002-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McCullough,_1776_2005_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCullough,_1776_2005-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A delegation including John Adams and Benjamin Franklin met <a href="/wiki/Richard_Howe,_1st_Earl_Howe" title="Richard Howe, 1st Earl Howe">Howe</a> on <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island" title="Staten Island">Staten Island</a> in New York Harbor on September 11, in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island_Peace_Conference" title="Staten Island Peace Conference">Staten Island Peace Conference</a>. Howe demanded a retraction of the Declaration of Independence, which was refused, and negotiations ended. The British then <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kip%27s_Bay" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Kip&#39;s Bay">quickly seized New York City</a> and nearly captured Washington\'s army. They made New York their main political and military base of operations in North America, <a href="/wiki/Evacuation_Day_(New_York)" title="Evacuation Day (New York)">holding it until November 1783</a>. The city became the destination for Loyalist refugees and a focal point of Washington\'s <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="Intelligence in the American Revolutionary War">intelligence network</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schecter,_Barnet_2002_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schecter,_Barnet_2002-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McCullough,_1776_2005_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCullough,_1776_2005-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British also took New Jersey, pushing the Continental Army into Pennsylvania. Washington <a href="/wiki/Washington%27s_crossing_of_the_Delaware" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington&#39;s crossing of the Delaware">crossed the Delaware River</a> back into New Jersey in a surprise attack in late December 1776 and defeated <a href="/wiki/Hessian_(soldier)" title="Hessian (soldier)">Hessian</a> and British armies at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton" title="Battle of Trenton">Trenton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Princeton" title="Battle of Princeton">Princeton</a>, thereby regaining control of most of New Jersey. The victories gave an important boost to Patriots at a time when morale was flagging, and have become iconic events of the war.\n</p><p>In 1777, the British sent Burgoyne\'s invasion force from Canada south to New York to seal off New England. Their aim was to neutralize the <a href="/wiki/Yankee#Early_usage" title="Yankee">Yankees</a>, whom the British perceived as the primary source of agitators. The British army in New York City went to Philadelphia in a major case of mis-coordination, capturing it from Washington. The invasion army under <a href="/wiki/John_Burgoyne" title="John Burgoyne">Burgoyne</a> was much too slow and became trapped in northern New York state. It surrendered after the <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Saratoga" title="Battles of Saratoga">Battles of Saratoga</a> in October 1777. From early October 1777 until November 15, a siege distracted British troops at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Mifflin" title="Fort Mifflin">Fort Mifflin</a>, Philadelphia, Pennsylvania and allowed Washington time to preserve the Continental Army by safely leading his troops to harsh winter quarters at <a href="/wiki/Valley_Forge" title="Valley Forge">Valley Forge</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prisoners">Prisoners</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="Prisoners of war in the American Revolutionary War">Prisoners of war in the American Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<p>In August 1775, George III declared Americans in arms against royal authority to be traitors to the Crown. Following their surrender at the <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Saratoga" title="Battles of Saratoga">Battles of Saratoga</a> in October 1777, there were thousands of British and Hessian soldiers in American hands. Although Lord Germain took a hard line, the British generals on the scene never held treason trials; they treated captured enemy soldiers as <a href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The dilemma was that tens of thousands of Loyalists were under American control and American retaliation would have been easy. The British built much of their strategy around using these Loyalists,<sup id="cite_ref-Larry_G._Bowman_1976_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Larry_G._Bowman_1976-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> and therefore, no Americans were put on trial for treason. The British maltreated the prisoners whom they held, resulting in more deaths to American sailors and soldiers than from combat operations.<sup id="cite_ref-Larry_G._Bowman_1976_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Larry_G._Bowman_1976-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the war, both sides released their surviving prisoners.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_alliances_after_1778">American alliances after 1778</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/France_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="France in the American Revolutionary War">France in the American Revolutionary War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Spain in the American Revolutionary War">Spain in the American Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<p>The capture of a British army at Saratoga encouraged the French to formally enter the war in support of Congress. Benjamin Franklin negotiated a permanent military alliance in early 1778, significantly becoming the first country to officially recognize the Declaration of Independence. On February 6, 1778, a <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Amity_and_Commerce_(USA%E2%80%93France)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Amity and Commerce (USA\xe2\x80\x93France)">Treaty of Amity and Commerce</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alliance_(1778)" title="Treaty of Alliance (1778)">Treaty of Alliance</a> were signed between the United States and France.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamilton,_1974_p._28_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton,_1974_p._28-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt,_1st_Earl_of_Chatham" title="William Pitt, 1st Earl of Chatham">William Pitt</a> spoke out in parliament urging Britain to make peace in America and to unite with America against France, while other British politicians who had previously sympathized with colonial grievances now turned against the Americans for allying with Britain\'s international rival and enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Later, Spain (in 1779) and the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch</a> (1780) became allies of the French, leaving the British Empire to fight a global war alone without major allies, and requiring it to slip through a combined blockade of the Atlantic. The American theater thus became only one front in Britain\'s war.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The British were forced to withdraw troops from continental America to reinforce the valuable sugar-producing Caribbean colonies, which were more lucrative to British investors.\n</p><p>British commander <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clinton_(American_War_of_Independence)" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Clinton (American War of Independence)">Sir Henry Clinton</a> evacuated Philadelphia and returned to New York City. General Washington intercepted Clinton in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Monmouth" title="Battle of Monmouth">Battle of Monmouth Court House</a>, the last major battle fought in the north. After an inconclusive engagement, the British successfully retreated to New York City. The northern war subsequently became a stalemate, as the focus of attention shifted to the smaller southern theater.<sup id="cite_ref-Higginbotham,_1983_p._83_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higginbotham,_1983_p._83-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Us_unabhaengigkeitskrieg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Us_unabhaengigkeitskrieg.jpg/250px-Us_unabhaengigkeitskrieg.jpg" width="250" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Us_unabhaengigkeitskrieg.jpg/375px-Us_unabhaengigkeitskrieg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Us_unabhaengigkeitskrieg.jpg/500px-Us_unabhaengigkeitskrieg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1050" data-file-height="793" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Us_unabhaengigkeitskrieg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center><a href="/wiki/Hessian_(soldiers)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hessian (soldiers)">Hessian</a> troops hired out to the British by their German sovereigns</center></div></div></div>\n<h3><span id="The_British_move_South.2C_1778.E2.80.931783"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_British_move_South,_1778\xe2\x80\x931783">The British move South, 1778\xe2\x80\x931783</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Southern_theater_of_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="Southern theater of the American Revolutionary War">Southern theater of the American Revolutionary War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naval_operations_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Naval operations in the American Revolutionary War">Naval operations in the American Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<p>The British strategy in America now concentrated on a campaign in the southern states. With fewer regular troops at their disposal, the British commanders saw the "southern strategy" as a more viable plan, as the south was perceived as being more strongly Loyalist, with a large population of recent immigrants as well as large numbers of slaves who might be captured or run away to join the British.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Beginning in late December 1778, the British captured <a href="/wiki/Savannah,_Georgia" title="Savannah, Georgia">Savannah</a> and controlled the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Georgia" title="Province of Georgia">Georgia</a> coastline. In 1780, they launched a fresh invasion and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Charleston" title="Siege of Charleston">took Charleston</a>, as well. A significant victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Camden" title="Battle of Camden">Battle of Camden</a> meant that royal forces soon controlled most of Georgia and South Carolina. The British set up a network of forts inland, hoping that the Loyalists would rally to the flag.<sup id="cite_ref-Henry_Lumpkin_2000_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henry_Lumpkin_2000-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Not enough Loyalists turned out, however, and the British had to fight their way north into North Carolina and Virginia, with a severely weakened army. Behind them, much of the territory that they had already captured dissolved into a chaotic <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Guerrilla war">guerrilla war</a>, fought predominantly between bands of Loyalist and American militia, which negated many of the gains that the British had previously made.<sup id="cite_ref-Henry_Lumpkin_2000_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henry_Lumpkin_2000-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Surrender_at_Yorktown_.281781.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Surrender_at_Yorktown_(1781)">Surrender at Yorktown (1781)</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown" title="Siege of Yorktown">Siege of Yorktown</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yorktown80.JPG" class="image"><img alt="The Siege of Yorktown ended with the surrender of a second British army, paving the way for the end of the American Revolutionary War" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Yorktown80.JPG/250px-Yorktown80.JPG" width="250" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Yorktown80.JPG/375px-Yorktown80.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Yorktown80.JPG/500px-Yorktown80.JPG 2x" data-file-width="734" data-file-height="567" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yorktown80.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown" title="Siege of Yorktown">siege of Yorktown</a> ended with the surrender of a second British army, marking effective British defeat.</center></div></div></div>\n<p>The British army under Cornwallis marched to <a href="/wiki/Yorktown,_Virginia" title="Yorktown, Virginia">Yorktown, Virginia</a> where they expected to be rescued by a British fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The fleet showed up, but so did a larger French fleet, so the British fleet returned to New York for reinforcements after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Chesapeake" title="Battle of the Chesapeake">Battle of the Chesapeake</a>, leaving Cornwallis trapped. In October 1781, the British surrendered their second invading army of the war, under a siege by the combined French and Continental armies commanded by Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_end_of_the_war">The end of the war</span></h3>\n<p>Historians continue to debate whether the odds for American victory were long or short. <a href="/wiki/John_E._Ferling" title="John E. Ferling">John E. Ferling</a> says that the odds were so long that the American victory was  "almost a miracle".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Ellis" title="Joseph Ellis">Joseph Ellis</a> says that the odds favored the Americans, and asks whether there ever was any realistic chance for the British to win. He argues that this opportunity came only once, in the summer of 1776, and the British failed that test.
