Admiral Howe and his brother General Howe "missed several opportunities to destroy the Continental Army.... Chance, luck, and even the vagaries of the weather played crucial roles." Ellis\'s point is that the strategic and tactical decisions of the Howes were fatally flawed because they underestimated the challenges posed by the Patriots. Ellis concludes that, once the Howe brothers failed, the opportunity for a British victory "would never come again."<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Support for the conflict had never been strong in Britain, where many sympathized with the Americans, but now it reached a new low.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> King George III personally wanted to fight on, but his supporters lost control of Parliament and no further major land offensives were launched in the American Theater.<sup id="cite_ref-Higginbotham,_1983_p._83_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higginbotham,_1983_p._83-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> It would be three decades until war resumed again with the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, which firmly established the permanence of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington could not know that the British would not reopen hostilities after Yorktown. They still had 26,000 troops occupying New York City, Charleston, and Savannah, together with a powerful fleet. The French army and navy departed, so the Americans were on their own in 1782\xe2\x80\x9383.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The treasury was empty, and the unpaid soldiers were growing restive, almost to the point of mutiny or possible <i>coup d\'\xc3\xa9tat</i>. The unrest among officers of the <a href="/wiki/Newburgh_Conspiracy" title="Newburgh Conspiracy">Newburgh Conspiracy</a> was personally dispelled by Washington in 1783, and Congress subsequently created the promise of a five years bonus for all officers.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paris_peace_treaty">Paris peace treaty</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris (1783)</a></div>\n<p>During negotiations in Paris, the American delegation discovered that France would support independence, but no territorial gains. The new nation would be confined to the area east of the Appalachian Mountains. The American delegation opened direct secret negotiations with London, cutting the French out. British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Petty,_2nd_Earl_of_Shelburne" title="William Petty, 2nd Earl of Shelburne">Lord Shelburne</a> was in full charge of the British negotiations. He now saw a chance to make the United States a valuable economic partner.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>  The US obtained all the land east of the Mississippi River, south of Canada, and north of Florida. It gained fishing rights off Canadian coasts, and agreed to allow British merchants and Loyalists to try to recover their property. It was a highly favorable treaty for the United States, and deliberately so from the British point of view. Prime Minister Shelburne foresaw highly profitable two-way trade between Britain and the rapidly growing United States, as indeed came to pass. Since the blockade was lifted and the old imperial restrictions were gone, American merchants were free to trade with any nation anywhere in the world, and their businesses flourished.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British largely abandoned the Indian allies living in the new nation. They were not a party to this treaty and did not recognize it until they were defeated militarily by the United States. However, the British did promise to support the Indians. They sold them munitions and maintained forts in American territory until the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> of 1795.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_on_Britain">Impact on Britain</span></h3>\n<p>Losing the war and the Thirteen Colonies was a shock to Britain. The war revealed the limitations of Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Fiscal-military_state" title="Fiscal-military state">fiscal-military state</a> when they discovered that they suddenly faced powerful enemies with no allies, and they were dependent on extended and vulnerable transatlantic lines of communication. The defeat heightened dissension and escalated political antagonism to the King\'s ministers. Inside Parliament, the primary concern changed from fears of an over-mighty monarch to the issues of representation, parliamentary reform, and government retrenchment. Reformers sought to destroy what they saw as widespread institutional corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-William_Hague_2004_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-William_Hague_2004-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jeremy_Black_2006_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeremy_Black_2006-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The result was a powerful crisis from 1776 to 1783. The peace in 1783 left France financially prostrate, while the British economy boomed thanks to the return of American business. The crisis ended after 1784 thanks to the King\'s shrewdness in outwitting <a href="/wiki/Charles_James_Fox" title="Charles James Fox">Charles James Fox</a> (the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Fox-North_Coalition" class="mw-redirect" title="Fox-North Coalition">Fox-North Coalition</a>), and renewed confidence in the system engendered by the leadership of the new Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt</a>. Historians conclude that loss of the American colonies enabled Britain to deal with the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> with more unity and better organization than would otherwise have been the case.<sup id="cite_ref-William_Hague_2004_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-William_Hague_2004-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jeremy_Black_2006_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeremy_Black_2006-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Britain turned towards Asia, the Pacific and later Africa with subsequent exploration leading to the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Second_British_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Second British Empire">Second British Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Finance">Finance</span></h2>\n<p>Britain\'s war against the Americans, French, and Spanish cost about \xc2\xa3100 million. The Treasury borrowed 40% of the money that it needed.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Heavy spending brought France to the <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_the_French_Revolution#Economics_and_finances" title="Causes of the French Revolution">verge of bankruptcy and revolution</a>, while the British had relatively little difficulty financing their war, keeping their suppliers and soldiers paid, and hiring tens of thousands of German soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Brewer_1990_p_91_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Brewer_1990_p_91-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britain had a sophisticated financial system based on the wealth of thousands of landowners, who supported the government, together with banks and financiers in London. The British tax system collected about 12 percent of the GDP in taxes during the 1770s.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Brewer_1990_p_91_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Brewer_1990_p_91-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In sharp contrast, Congress and the American states had no end of difficulty financing the war.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In 1775, there was at most 12 million dollars in gold in the colonies, not nearly enough to cover current transactions, let alone finance a major war. The British made the situation much worse by imposing a tight blockade on every American port, which cut off almost all imports and exports. One partial solution was to rely on volunteer support from militiamen and donations from patriotic citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-Charles_Rappleye_2010_pp_225_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charles_Rappleye_2010_pp_225-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Another was to delay actual payments, pay soldiers and suppliers in depreciated currency, and promise that it would be made good after the war. Indeed, the soldiers and officers were given land grants in 1783 to cover the wages that they had earned but had not been paid during the war. Not until 1781 did the national government have a strong leader in financial matters, when <a href="/wiki/Robert_Morris_(financier)" title="Robert Morris (financier)">Robert Morris</a> was named <a href="/wiki/Superintendent_of_Finance_of_the_United_States" title="Superintendent of Finance of the United States">Superintendent of Finance of the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Charles_Rappleye_2010_pp_225_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charles_Rappleye_2010_pp_225-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Morris used a French loan in 1782 to set up the private <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_North_America" title="Bank of North America">Bank of North America</a> to finance the war. Seeking greater efficiency, Morris reduced the civil list, saved money by using competitive bidding for contracts, tightened accounting procedures, and demanded the national government\'s full share of money and supplies from the confederated states.<sup id="cite_ref-Charles_Rappleye_2010_pp_225_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charles_Rappleye_2010_pp_225-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Congress used four main methods to cover the cost of the war, which cost about 66 million dollars in specie (gold and silver).<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Congress made two issues of paper money\xe2\x80\x94in 1775\xe2\x80\x931780 and in 1780\xe2\x80\x9381. The first issue amounted to 242 million dollars. This paper money would supposedly be redeemed for state taxes, but the holders were eventually paid off in 1791 at the rate of one cent on the dollar. By 1780, the paper money was "not worth a Continental", as people said.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The skyrocketing inflation was a hardship on the few people who had fixed incomes\xe2\x80\x94but 90 percent of the people were farmers, and were not directly affected by that inflation. Debtors benefited by paying off their debts with depreciated paper. The greatest burden was borne by the soldiers of the Continental Army, whose wages were usually in arrears and declined in value every month, weakening their morale and adding to the hardships of their families.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Beginning in 1777, Congress repeatedly asked the states to provide money. But the states had no system of taxation either, and were little help. By 1780, Congress was making requisitions for specific supplies of corn, beef, pork, and other necessities\xe2\x80\x94an inefficient system that kept the army barely alive.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Starting in 1776, the Congress sought to raise money by loans from wealthy individuals, promising to redeem the bonds after the war. The bonds were in fact redeemed in 1791 at face value, but the scheme raised little money because Americans had little specie, and many of the rich merchants were supporters of the Crown. Starting in 1776, the French secretly supplied the Americans with money, gunpowder, and munitions in order to weaken its arch enemy Great Britain. When France officially entered the war in 1778, the subsidies continued, and the French government, as well as bankers in Paris and Amsterdam, lent large sums to the American war effort. These loans were repaid in full in the 1790s.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Concluding_the_Revolution">Concluding the Revolution</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia Convention">Philadelphia Convention</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">United States Bill of Rights</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Annapolis_Convention_(1786)" title="Annapolis Convention (1786)">Annapolis Convention (1786)</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Federalist_Papers" title="The Federalist Papers">The Federalist Papers</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Creating_a_.22more_perfect_union.22_and_guaranteeing_rights"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Creating_a_&quot;more_perfect_union&quot;_and_guaranteeing_rights">Creating a "more perfect union" and guaranteeing rights</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Annapolis_Convention_(1786)" title="Annapolis Convention (1786)">Annapolis Convention (1786)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">United States Bill of Rights</a></div>\n<p>The war finally ended in 1783 and was followed by a period of prosperity. The national government was still operating under the Articles of Confederation and was able to settle the issue of the western territories, which were ceded by the states to Congress. American settlers moved rapidly into those areas, with Vermont, Kentucky, and Tennessee becoming states in the 1790s.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the national government had no money to pay either the war debts owed to European nations and the private banks, or to pay Americans who had been given millions of dollars of promissory notes for supplies during the war. Nationalists led by Washington, Alexander Hamilton, and other veterans feared that the new nation was too fragile to withstand an international war, or even internal revolts such as the <a href="/wiki/Shays%27_Rebellion" title="Shays&#39; Rebellion">Shays\' Rebellion</a> of 1786 in Massachusetts.\n</p><p>Calling themselves "Federalists," the nationalists convinced Congress to call the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia Convention">Philadelphia Convention</a> in 1787.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> It adopted a new <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Constitution of the United States">Constitution</a> that provided for a much stronger federal government, including an effective executive in a <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">check-and-balance</a> system with the judiciary and legislature.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The Constitution was ratified in 1788, after a fierce debate in the states over the nature of the proposed new government. The new government under President George Washington took office in New York in March 1789.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Amendments to the Constitution were spearheaded in Congress by <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> as assurances to those who were cautious about federal power, guaranteeing many of the <a href="/wiki/Inalienable_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Inalienable rights">inalienable rights</a> that formed a foundation for the revolution. The amendments were ratified by the states in 1791.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_debt">National debt</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/United_States_public_debt" class="mw-redirect" title="United States public debt">United States public debt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a></div>\n<p>The national debt fell into three categories after the American Revolution. The first was the $12 million owed to foreigners, mostly money borrowed from France. There was general agreement to pay the foreign debts at full value. The national government owed $40 million and state governments owed $25 million to Americans who had sold food, horses, and supplies to the revolutionary forces. There were also other debts that consisted of <a href="/wiki/Promissory_note" title="Promissory note">promissory notes</a> issued during the Revolutionary War to soldiers, merchants, and farmers who accepted these payments on the premise that the new Constitution would create a government that would pay these debts eventually.\n</p><p>The war expenses of the individual states added up to $114 million compared to $37 million by the central government.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In 1790, Congress combined the remaining state debts with the foreign and domestic debts into one national debt totaling $80 million at the recommendation of first Secretary of the Treasury Alexander Hamilton. Everyone received face value for wartime certificates, so that the national honor would be sustained and the national credit established.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology_and_factions">Ideology and factions</span></h2>\n<p>The population of the 13 Colonies was not homogeneous in their political views and attitudes. Loyalties and allegiances varied widely within regions and communities and even within families, and sometimes shifted during the course of the Revolution.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology_behind_the_Revolution">Ideology behind the Revolution</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Enlightenment" title="American Enlightenment">American Enlightenment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Liberalism in the United States">Liberalism in the United States</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">Republicanism in the United States</a></div>\n<p>The American Enlightenment was a critical precursor of the American Revolution. Chief among the ideas of the American Enlightenment were the concepts of Natural Law, Natural Rights, Consent of the Governed, Individualism, Property Rights, Self-Ownership, Self-Determination, liberalism, republicanism and fear of corruption. Collectively, the acceptance of these concepts by a growing number of American colonists began to foster an intellectual environment which would lead to a new sense of political and social identity.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberalism">Liberalism</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Liberalism in the United States">Liberalism in the United States</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Social_Contract" class="mw-redirect" title="Social Contract">Social Contract</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_Rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural Rights">Natural Rights</a></div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Liberalism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:J_S_Copley_-_Samuel_Adams.jpg" class="image"><img alt="John Adams is a stern middle-aged man with gray hair is wearing a dark red suit. He is standing behind a table, holding a rolled up document in one hand, and pointing with the other hand to a large document on the table." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/J_S_Copley_-_Samuel_Adams.jpg/220px-J_S_Copley_-_Samuel_Adams.jpg" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/J_S_Copley_-_Samuel_Adams.jpg/330px-J_S_Copley_-_Samuel_Adams.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/J_S_Copley_-_Samuel_Adams.jpg/440px-J_S_Copley_-_Samuel_Adams.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="775" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:J_S_Copley_-_Samuel_Adams.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>In this <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1772</span> portrait by <a href="/wiki/John_Singleton_Copley" title="John Singleton Copley">John Singleton Copley</a>, Samuel Adams points at the <a href="/wiki/Explanatory_charter" class="mw-redirect" title="Explanatory charter">Massachusetts Charter</a>, which he viewed as a constitution that protected the people\'s rights.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></center></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>\'s (1632\xe2\x80\x931704) ideas on liberty influenced the political thinking behind the revolution, especially through his indirect influence on English writers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Trenchard_(writer)" title="John Trenchard (writer)">John Trenchard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gordon_(writer)" title="Thomas Gordon (writer)">Thomas Gordon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Hoadly" title="Benjamin Hoadly">Benjamin Hoadly</a>, whose political ideas in turn had a strong influence on the American revolutionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Locke is often referred to as "the philosopher of the American Revolution" due to his work in the Social Contract and Natural Rights theories that underpinned the Revolution\'s political ideology.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Locke\'s <a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a> published in 1689 were especially influential. He argued that all humans were created equally free, and governments therefore needed the "consent of the governed."<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>  In late eighteenth-century America, belief was still widespread in "equality by creation" and "rights by creation".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The theory of the "<a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a>" influenced the belief among many of the Founders that among the "natural rights" of man was the <a href="/wiki/Right_of_revolution" title="Right of revolution">right of the people to overthrow their leaders</a>, should those leaders betray the historic <a href="/wiki/Rights_of_Englishmen" title="Rights of Englishmen">rights of Englishmen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Toth1989_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toth1989-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cohen2008_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2008-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In terms of writing state and national constitutions, the Americans heavily used <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Secondat,_baron_de_Montesquieu" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles de Secondat, baron de Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>\'s analysis of the wisdom of the "balanced" British Constitution (<a href="/wiki/Mixed_government" title="Mixed government">mixed government</a>).\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Republicanism">Republicanism</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">Republicanism in the United States</a></div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks hlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><td style="padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Politics" title="Category:Politics">the Politics series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;font-size: 200%; font-weight: normal;"><a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">Republicanism</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;padding:0 0 1.4em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Consul_et_lictores.png" class="image"><img alt="Consul et lictores.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Consul_et_lictores.png/100px-Consul_et_lictores.png" publica">Res publica</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">Social contract</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div text-align: center; padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 2.4em;">Schools</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a thinkers</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hannah_Arendt" title="Hannah Arendt">Hannah Arendt</a></li>\n<li><a Sandel</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Algernon_Sidney" title="Algernon Sidney">Algernon Sidney</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Quentin_Skinner" title="Quentin Skinner">Quentin Skinner</a></li>\n<li><a Wollstonecraft</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a></li>\n<li><a
