War">Nepalese Civil War</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:transparent; border-top: 1px solid #aaa; text-align: center; padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 2.4em;">By country</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_Australia" title="Republicanism in Australia">Australia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_Barbados" title="Republicanism in Barbados">Barbados</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_Canada" title="Republicanism in Canada">Canada</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">Ireland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_Jamaica" title="Republicanism in Jamaica">Jamaica</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_Morocco" title="Republicanism in Morocco">Morocco</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_Netherlands" title="Republicanism in the Netherlands">Netherlands</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_New_Zealand" title="Republicanism in New Zealand">New Zealand</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_Spain" title="Republicanism in Spain">Spain</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_Sweden" title="Republicanism in Sweden">Sweden</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_Turkey" title="Republicanism in Turkey">Turkey</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Republicanism in the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">United States</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:transparent; border-top: 1px solid #aaa; text-align: center; padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 2.4em;">Related topics</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Communitarianism" title="Communitarianism">Communitarianism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">Liberalism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Monarchism" title="Monarchism">Monarchism</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;text-align: center;">\n<span style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></span></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Republicanism_sidebar" title="Template:Republicanism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Republicanism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Republicanism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Republicanism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>A central motivating force behind the overthrow of monarchy and aristocracy was the American embrace of a political ideology called "republicanism",<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> which was dominant in the colonies by 1775 but of minor importance back in Great Britain.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The republicanism was inspired by the "<a href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whig (British political party)">country party</a>" in Great Britain, whose critique of British government emphasized that <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">corruption</a> was a terrible reality in Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Weintraub2005_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weintraub2005-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Americans feared that the corruption was crossing the Atlantic; the commitment of most Americans to republican values and to their rights energized the revolution, as Britain was increasingly seen as hopelessly corrupt and hostile to American interests. Britain seemed to threaten the established liberties that Americans enjoyed.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The greatest threat to liberty was depicted as corruption\xe2\x80\x94not just in London but at home, as well. The colonists associated it with luxury, and especially with inherited aristocracy, which they condemned.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Founding Fathers were strong advocates of republican values, particularly <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Adams" title="Samuel Adams">Samuel Adams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Henry" title="Patrick Henry">Patrick Henry</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> which required men to put civic duty ahead of their personal desires. Men had a civic duty to be prepared and willing to fight for the rights and liberties of their countrymen. John Adams wrote to <a href="/wiki/Mercy_Otis_Warren" title="Mercy Otis Warren">Mercy Otis Warren</a> in 1776, agreeing with some <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical</a> Greek and Roman thinkers in that "Public Virtue cannot exist without private, and public Virtue is the only Foundation of Republics." He continued:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There must be a positive Passion for the public good, the public Interest, Honour, Power, and Glory, established in the Minds of the People, or there can be no Republican Government, nor any real Liberty. And this public Passion must be Superior to all private Passions. Men must be ready, they must pride themselves, and be happy to sacrifice their private Pleasures, Passions, and Interests, nay their private Friendships and dearest connections, when they Stand in Competition with the Rights of society.<sup id="cite_ref-Rahe1994_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rahe1994-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>For women, "<a href="/wiki/Republican_motherhood" title="Republican motherhood">republican motherhood</a>" became the ideal, exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mercy_Otis_Warren" title="Mercy Otis Warren">Mercy Otis Warren</a>; the first duty of the republican woman was to instill republican values in her children and to avoid luxury and ostentation.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Fusing_republicanism_and_liberalism">Fusing republicanism and liberalism</span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Commonsense.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Thomas Paine&#39;s pamphlet Common Sense, published in 1776" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Commonsense.jpg/220px-Commonsense.jpg" width="220" height="345" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Commonsense.jpg/330px-Commonsense.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Commonsense.jpg/440px-Commonsense.jpg 2x" data-file-width="510" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Commonsense.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>\'s pamphlet <i><a href="/wiki/Common_Sense_(pamphlet)" title="Common Sense (pamphlet)">Common Sense</a></i>, published in 1776</center></div></div></div>\n<p>Some republics had emerged throughout history, such as the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> of the ancient world, but none ever existed that was based on liberal principles. Thomas Paine\'s pamphlet <i><a href="/wiki/Common_Sense_(pamphlet)" title="Common Sense (pamphlet)">Common Sense</a></i> appeared in January 1776, after the Revolution had started. It was widely distributed and loaned, and often read aloud in <a href="/wiki/Tavern#North_America" title="Tavern">taverns</a>, contributing significantly to spreading the ideas of republicanism and liberalism together, bolstering enthusiasm for separation from Great Britain, and encouraging recruitment for the Continental Army.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson,_2000_pp._465_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson,_2000_pp._465-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Paine provided a new and widely accepted argument for independence by advocating a complete break with history. <i>Common Sense</i> is oriented to the future in a way that compels the reader to make an immediate choice. It offered a solution for Americans disgusted and alarmed at the threat of tyranny.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson,_2000_pp._465_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson,_2000_pp._465-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_Great_Awakening">Impact of Great Awakening</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Great_Awakening" title="First Great Awakening">First Great Awakening</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">Dissenting churches of the day</a> (Protestant, non-Church of England) were the "school of democracy", in the words of Patricia Bonomi.<sup id="cite_ref-Bonomi_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bonomi-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/John_Witherspoon" title="John Witherspoon">John Witherspoon</a> of the College of New Jersey (now <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>) wrote widely circulated sermons linking the American Revolution to the teachings of the Bible. Throughout the colonies, dissenting Protestant ministers (<a href="/wiki/Congregationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Congregationalist">Congregationalist</a>, Baptist, and Presbyterian) preached Revolutionary themes in their sermons, while most Church of England clergymen preached loyalty to the king, the titular head of the English state church.<sup id="cite_ref-Nelson1961_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson1961-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Religious motivation for fighting tyranny transcended socioeconomic lines to encompass rich and poor, men and women, frontiersmen and townsmen, farmers and merchants.<sup id="cite_ref-Bonomi_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bonomi-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Declaration also referred to the "Laws of Nature and of Nature\'s God" as justification for the Americans\' separation from the British monarchy. Most eighteenth-century Americans believed that nature, the entire universe, was God\'s creation<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> and he was "Nature\'s God". Everything, including man, was part of the "universal order of things", which began with God and was directed by his providence.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Accordingly, the signers of the Declaration professed their "firm reliance on the Protection of divine Providence", and they appealed to "the Supreme Judge for the rectitude of our intentions".<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Like most of his countrymen, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> was firmly convinced that he was an instrument of providence, to the benefit of the American people and of all humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Bailyn" title="Bernard Bailyn">Bernard Bailyn</a> argues that the evangelicalism of the era challenged traditional notions of natural hierarchy by preaching that the Bible teaches that all men are equal, so that the true value of a man lies in his moral behavior, not in his class.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Kidd argues that religious disestablishment, belief in God as the source of human rights, and shared convictions about sin, virtue, and divine providence worked together to unite rationalists and evangelicals and thus encouraged American defiance of the Empire. Bailyn, on the other hand, denies that religion played such a critical role.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_S._Kidd_2010_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_S._Kidd_2010-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Alan Heimert argues that New Light anti-authoritarianism was essential to furthering democracy in colonial American society and set the stage for a confrontation with British monarchical and aristocratic rule.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_clergy_in_the_American_Revolution" title="List of clergy in the American Revolution">List of clergy in the American Revolution</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Class_and_psychology_of_the_factions">Class and psychology of the factions</span></h3>\n<p>Looking back, John Adams concluded in 1818:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Revolution was effected before the war commenced. The Revolution was in the minds and hearts of the people&#160;... This radical change in the principles, opinions, sentiments, and affections of the people was the real American Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In terms of class, Loyalists tended to have longstanding social and economic connections to British merchants and government; for instance, prominent merchants in major port cities such as New York, Boston and Charleston tended to be Loyalists, as did men involved with the <a href="/wiki/Fur_trade" title="Fur trade">fur trade</a> along the northern frontier.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In addition, officials of colonial government and their staffs, those who had established positions and status to maintain, favored maintaining relations with Great Britain. They often were linked to British families in England by marriage as well.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>By contrast, Patriots by number tended to be <a href="/wiki/Yeoman#United_States" title="Yeoman">yeomen farmers</a>, especially in the frontier areas of New York and the backcountry of Pennsylvania, Virginia and down the Appalachian mountains.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> They were craftsmen and small merchants. Leaders of both the Patriots and the Loyalists were men of educated, propertied classes. The Patriots included many prominent men of the <a href="/wiki/Planter_(American_South)" class="mw-redirect" title="Planter (American South)">planter class</a> from Virginia and South Carolina, for instance, who became leaders during the Revolution, and formed the new government at the national and state levels.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>To understand the opposing groups, historians have assessed evidence of their hearts and minds. In the mid-20th century, historian <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Woods_Labaree" title="Leonard Woods Labaree">Leonard Woods Labaree</a> identified eight characteristics of the Loyalists that made them essentially conservative; opposite traits to those characteristic of the Patriots.<sup id="cite_ref-Labaree,_1948_pp._164\xe2\x80\x9365_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labaree,_1948_pp._164\xe2\x80\x9365-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Older and better established men, Loyalists tended to resist innovation. They thought resistance to the Crown\xe2\x80\x94which they insisted was the only legitimate government\xe2\x80\x94was morally wrong, while the Patriots thought morality was on their side.<sup id="cite_ref-Hull_1978_pp._344_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hull_1978_pp._344-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wallace,_1972_pp._167_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace,_1972_pp._167-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Loyalists were alienated when the Patriots resorted to violence, such as burning houses and <a href="/wiki/Tarring_and_feathering" title="Tarring and feathering">tarring and feathering</a>. Loyalists wanted to take a centrist position and resisted the Patriots\' demand to declare their opposition to the Crown. Many Loyalists, especially merchants in the port cities, had maintained strong and long-standing relations with Britain (often with business and family links to other parts of the British Empire).<sup id="cite_ref-Hull_1978_pp._344_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hull_1978_pp._344-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wallace,_1972_pp._167_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace,_1972_pp._167-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many Loyalists realized that independence was bound to come eventually, but they were fearful that revolution might lead to anarchy, tyranny or mob rule. In contrast, the prevailing attitude among Patriots, who made systematic efforts to use mob violence in a controlled manner, was a desire to seize the initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-Hull_1978_pp._344_131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hull_1978_pp._344-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wallace,_1972_pp._167_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace,_1972_pp._167-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Labaree also wrote that Loyalists were pessimists who lacked the confidence in the future displayed by the Patriots.<sup id="cite_ref-Labaree,_1948_pp._164\xe2\x80\x9365_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labaree,_1948_pp._164\xe2\x80\x9365-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historians in the early 20th century, such as <a href="/wiki/J._Franklin_Jameson" title="J. Franklin Jameson">J. Franklin Jameson</a>, examined the class composition of the Patriot cause, looking for evidence of a class war inside the revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> In the last 50&#160;years, historians have largely abandoned that interpretation, emphasizing instead the high level of ideological unity.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Just as there were rich and poor Loyalists, the Patriots were a \'mixed lot\', with the richer and better educated more likely to become officers in the Army.<sup id="cite_ref-Nash_2005_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nash_2005-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resch_2006_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resch_2006-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ideological demands always came first: the Patriots viewed independence as a means to gain freedom from British oppression and taxation and, above all, to reassert what they considered to be their rights as English subjects. Most yeomen farmers, craftsmen, and small merchants joined the Patriot cause to demand more political equality. They were especially successful in Pennsylvania but less so in New England, where John Adams attacked Thomas Paine\'s <i>Common Sense</i> for the "absurd democratical notions" it proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-Nash_2005_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nash_2005-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resch_2006_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resch_2006-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="King_George_III">King George III</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_Great_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="George III of Great Britain">George III of Great Britain</a></div>\n<p>The war became a personal issue for <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">the king</a>, fueled by his growing belief that British leniency would be taken as weakness by the Americans. The king also sincerely believed he was defending Britain\'s constitution against usurpers, rather than opposing patriots fighting for their natural rights.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patriots">Patriots</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Patriot_(American_Revolution)" title="Patriot (American Revolution)">Patriot (American Revolution)</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_Liberty" title="Sons of Liberty">Sons of Liberty</a></div>\n<p>The revolutionaries were called "Patriots", "Whigs", "Congress-men", or "Americans" during and after the war. They included a full range of social and economic classes but were unanimous regarding the need to defend the rights of Americans and uphold the principles of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republicanism</a> in terms of rejecting monarchy and aristocracy, while emphasizing civic virtue on the part of the citizens. Newspapers were strongholds of patriotism (although there were a few Loyalist papers) and printed many pamphlets, announcements, patriotic letters, and pronouncements.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to historian Robert Calhoon, 40\xe2\x80\x9345% of the white population in the Thirteen Colonies supported the Patriots\' cause, 15\xe2\x80\x9320% supported the Loyalists, and the remainder were neutral or kept a low profile.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Mark Lender analyzes why ordinary people became insurgents against the British, even if they were unfamiliar with the ideological reasons behind the war. He concludes that such people held a sense of rights which the British were violating, rights that stressed local autonomy, fair dealing, and government by consent. They were highly sensitive to the issue of tyranny, which they saw manifested in the British response to the Boston Tea Party. The arrival in Boston of the British Army heightened their sense of violated rights, leading to rage and demands for revenge. They had faith that God was on their side.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loyalists">Loyalists</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution)" title="Loyalist (American Revolution)">Loyalist (American Revolution)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mobbing_the_Tories_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16960.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Mobbing_the_Tories_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16960.jpg/250px-Mobbing_the_Tories_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16960.jpg" width="250" height="336" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/Mobbing_the_Tories_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16960.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="290" data-file-height="390" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mobbing_the_Tories_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16960.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Mobbing of a Loyalist by American Patriots in 1775\xe2\x80\x9376</center></div></div></div>\n<p>The consensus of scholars is that about 15\xe2\x80\x9320% of the white population remained loyal to the British Crown.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Those who actively supported the king were known at the time as "Loyalists", "Tories", or "King\'s men". The Loyalists never controlled territory unless the British Army occupied it. They were typically older, less willing to break with old loyalties, and often connected to the Church of England; they included many established merchants with strong business connections throughout the Empire, as well as royal officials such as Thomas Hutchinson of Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> There were 500 to 1,000 <a href="/wiki/Black_Loyalist" title="Black Loyalist">black loyalists</a>, slaves who escaped to British lines and joined the British army. Most died of disease, but Britain took the survivors to Canada as free men.\n</p><p>The revolution could divide families, such as <a href="/wiki/William_Franklin" title="William Franklin">William Franklin</a>, son of Benjamin Franklin and royal governor of the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_New_Jersey" title="Province of New Jersey">Province of New Jersey</a> who remained loyal to the Crown throughout the war. He and his father never spoke again.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Recent immigrants who had not been fully Americanized were also inclined to support the King, such as <a href="/wiki/Flora_MacDonald_(Scottish_Jacobite)" class="mw-redirect" title="Flora MacDonald (Scottish Jacobite)">Flora MacDonald</a> who was a Scottish settler in the back country.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the war, the great majority of the approximately 500,000 Loyalists remained in America and resumed normal lives. Some became prominent American leaders, such as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Seabury_(1729%E2%80%931796)" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel Seabury (1729\xe2\x80\x931796)">Samuel Seabury</a>. Approximately 62,000 Loyalists relocated to Canada; others moved to Britain (7,000), Florida, or the West Indies (9,000). The exiles represented approximately two percent of the total population of the colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-Pole_1994_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pole_1994-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Nearly all black loyalists left for Nova Scotia, Florida, or England, where they could remain free.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Loyalists who left the South in 1783 took thousands of their slaves with them to be slaves in the British West Indies.<sup id="cite_ref-Pole_1994_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pole_1994-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neutrals">Neutrals</span></h3>\n<p>A minority of uncertain size tried to stay neutral in the war. Most kept a low profile, but the <a href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious Society of Friends">Quakers</a> were the most important group to speak out for neutrality, especially in Pennsylvania. The Quakers continued to do business with the British even after the war began, and they were accused of being supporters of British rule, "contrivers and authors of seditious publications" critical of the revolutionary cause.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The majority of Quakers attempted to remain neutral, although a sizable number of <a href="/wiki/Quakers_in_the_American_Revolution" title="Quakers in the American Revolution">Quakers in the American Revolution</a> nevertheless participated to some degree.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_women">Role of women</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abigail_Adams.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Abigail Adams" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Abigail_Adams.jpg/220px-Abigail_Adams.jpg" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Abigail_Adams.jpg/330px-Abigail_Adams.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Abigail_Adams.jpg/440px-Abigail_Adams.jpg 2x" data-file-width="711" data-file-height="936" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abigail_Adams.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center><a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail Adams</a></center></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_American_Revolution" title="Women in the American Revolution">Women in the American Revolution</a></div>\n<p>Women contributed to the American Revolution in many ways, and were involved on both sides. While formal Revolutionary politics did not include women, ordinary domestic behaviors became charged with political significance as Patriot women confronted a war that permeated all aspects of political, civil, and domestic life. They participated by boycotting British goods, spying on the British, following armies as they marched, washing, cooking, and tending for soldiers, delivering secret messages, and in a few cases like <a href="/wiki/Deborah_Samson" class="mw-redirect" title="Deborah Samson">Deborah Samson</a>, fighting disguised as men. Also, <a href="/wiki/Mercy_Otis_Warren" title="Mercy Otis Warren">Mercy Otis Warren</a> held meetings in her house and cleverly attacked Loyalists with her creative plays and histories.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Above all, they continued the agricultural work at home to feed their families and the armies. They maintained their families during their husbands\' absences and sometimes after their deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-Berkin,_2006_pp._59\xe2\x80\x9360_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berkin,_2006_pp._59\xe2\x80\x9360-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>American women were integral to the success of the boycott of British goods,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> as the boycotted items were largely household items such as tea and cloth. Women had to return to knitting goods, and to spinning and weaving their own cloth \xe2\x80\x94 skills that had fallen into disuse. In 1769, the women of Boston produced 40,000 skeins of yarn, and 180 women in <a href="/wiki/Middleton,_Massachusetts" title="Middleton, Massachusetts">Middletown, Massachusetts</a> wove 20,522 yards (18,765&#160;m) of cloth.<sup id="cite_ref-Berkin,_2006_pp._59\xe2\x80\x9360_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berkin,_2006_pp._59\xe2\x80\x9360-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A crisis of political loyalties could disrupt the fabric of colonial America women\'s social worlds: whether a man did or did not renounce his allegiance to the King could dissolve ties of class, family, and friendship, isolating women from former connections. A woman\'s loyalty to her husband, once a private commitment, could become a political act, especially for women in America committed to men who remained loyal to the King. Legal divorce, usually rare, was granted to Patriot women whose husbands supported the King.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_participants">Other participants</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="Diplomacy in the American Revolutionary War">Diplomacy in the American Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/France_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="France in the American Revolutionary War">France in the American Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<p>In early 1776, France set up a major program of aid to the Americans, and the Spanish secretly added funds. Each country spent one million "livres tournaises" to buy munitions. A <a href="/wiki/Dummy_corporation" title="Dummy corporation">dummy corporation</a> run by <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Beaumarchais" title="Pierre Beaumarchais">Pierre Beaumarchais</a> concealed their activities. American rebels obtained some munitions through the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> as well as French and Spanish ports in the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spain_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Spain in the American Revolutionary War">Spain in the American Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<p>Spain did not officially recognize the U.S. but became an informal ally when it declared war on Britain on June 21, 1779. <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_de_G%C3%A1lvez_y_Madrid" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernardo de G\xc3\xa1lvez y Madrid">Bernardo de G\xc3\xa1lvez y Madrid</a>, general of the Spanish forces in <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a>, also served as governor of Louisiana. He led an expedition of colonial troops to force the British out of Florida and keep open a vital conduit for supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Indians">American Indians</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans in the United States</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Western_theater_of_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="Western theater of the American Revolutionary War">Western theater of the American Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<p>Most American Indians rejected pleas that they remain neutral and instead supported the British Crown.  The great majority of the 200,000 Indians east of the Mississippi distrusted the Colonists and supported the British cause, hoping to forestall continued colonial encroachment on their territories.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Those tribes that were more closely involved in trade tended to side with the Patriots, although political factors were important, as well.\n</p><p>Most Indians did not participate directly in the war, except for warriors and bands associated with four of the <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Iroquois</a> nations in New York and Pennsylvania which allied with the British. The British did have other allies, especially in the upper Midwest. They provided Indians with funding and weapons to attack American outposts. Some Indians tried to remain neutral, seeing little value in joining what they perceived to be a European conflict, and fearing reprisals from whichever side they opposed. The <a href="/wiki/Oneida_tribe" class="mw-redirect" title="Oneida tribe">Oneida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuscarora_people" title="Tuscarora people">Tuscarora</a> among the Iroquois of central and western New York supported the American cause.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British provided arms to Indians who were led by <a href="/wiki/Loyalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Loyalists">Loyalists</a> in war parties to raid frontier settlements from the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Carolina" title="Province of Carolina">Carolinas</a> to New York. They killed many settlers on the frontier, especially in Pennsylvania and New York\'s Mohawk Valley.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1776, <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a> war parties attacked American Colonists all along the southern frontier of the uplands throughout the <a href="/wiki/Washington_District,_North_Carolina" title="Washington District, North Carolina">Washington District, North Carolina</a> (now Tennessee) and the Kentucky wilderness area.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> They would launch raids with roughly 200 warriors, as seen in the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee%E2%80%93American_wars" title="Cherokee\xe2\x80\x93American wars">Cherokee\xe2\x80\x93American wars</a>; they could not mobilize enough forces to invade Colonial areas without the help of allies, most often the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee_(Creek)" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscogee (Creek)">Creek</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Chickamauga_Cherokee" title="Chickamauga Cherokee">Chickamauga Cherokee</a> under <a href="/wiki/Dragging_Canoe" title="Dragging Canoe">Dragging Canoe</a> allied themselves closely with the British, and fought on for an additional decade after the signing of the Treaty of Paris.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Brant" title="Joseph Brant">Joseph Brant</a> of the powerful <a href="/wiki/Mohawk_nation" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohawk nation">Mohawk nation</a>, part of the Iroquois Confederacy based in New York, was the most prominent Indian leader against the Colonial forces. In 1778 and 1780, he led 300 Iroquois warriors and 100 white Loyalists in multiple attacks on small frontier settlements in New York and Pennsylvania, killing many settlers and destroying villages, crops, and stores.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The Seneca, Onondaga, and Cayuga of the Iroquois Confederacy also allied with the British against the Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-Colin_G._Calloway_1995_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colin_G._Calloway_1995-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1779, the Colonists forced the hostile Indians out of upstate New York when Washington sent an army under <a href="/wiki/John_Sullivan_(general)" title="John Sullivan (general)">John Sullivan</a> which destroyed 40 empty Iroquois villages in central and western New York. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Newtown" title="Battle of Newtown">Battle of Newtown</a> proved decisive as the Patriots had a 3-1 advantage and ended significant resistance. Otherwise there was little combat. Sullivan systematically burned the villages and destroyed about 160,000 bushels of corn that composed the winter food supply. Facing starvation and homeless for the winter, the Iroquois fled to Canada. The British resettled them in Ontario, providing land grants as compensation for some of their losses.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the peace conference following the war, the British ceded lands which they did not really control, and did not consult their Indian allies. They transferred control to the United States of all the land east of the Mississippi and north of Florida. Calloway concludes:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Burned villages and crops, murdered chiefs, divided councils and civil wars, migrations, towns and forts choked with refugees, economic disruption, breaking of ancient traditions, losses in battle and to disease and hunger, betrayal to their enemies, all made the American Revolution one of the darkest periods in American Indian history.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The British did not give up their forts in the West until 1796 in what is now the eastern Midwest, stretching from Ohio to Wisconsin; they kept alive the dream of forming a satellite Indian nation there, which they called a Neutral Indian Zone. That goal was one of the causes of the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_Americans">Black Americans</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/African_Americans_in_the_Revolutionary_War" title="African Americans in the Revolutionary War">African Americans in the Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<p>Free blacks in the North and South fought on both sides of the Revolution, but <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_African_Americans#Revolutionary_War" title="Military history of African Americans">most fought for the Patriots</a>. Gary Nash reports that there were about 9,000 black Patriots, counting the Continental Army and Navy, state militia units, privateers, wagoneers in the Army, servants to officers, and spies.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Ray Raphael notes that thousands did join the Loyalist cause, but "a far larger number, free as well as slave, tried to further their interests by siding with the patriots."<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crispus_Attucks" title="Crispus Attucks">Crispus Attucks</a> was shot dead by British soldiers in the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Massacre" title="Boston Massacre">Boston Massacre</a> in 1770 and is an iconic martyr to Patriots.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Both sides offered freedom and re-settlement to slaves who were willing to fight for them, recruiting slaves whose owners supported the opposing cause.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Did the patriots do this? This article includes citations for the British having done this, but I don&#39;t see anything here that the patriots did. I believe a few slave owners probably did, but those were too rare to justify this sweeping claim. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Many black slaves sided with the Loyalists. Tens of thousands in the South used the turmoil of war to escape, and the southern plantation economies of South Carolina and Georgia especially were disrupted. During the Revolution, the British tried to turn slavery against the Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-Revolutionary_War:_The_Home_Front_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Revolutionary_War:_The_Home_Front-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/David_Brion_Davis" title="David Brion Davis">David Brion Davis</a> explains the difficulties with a policy of wholesale arming of the slaves:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>But England greatly feared the effects of any such move on its own <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a>, where Americans had already aroused alarm over a possible threat to incite slave <a href="/wiki/Insurrection" class="mw-redirect" title="Insurrection">insurrections</a>. The British elites also understood that an all-out attack on one form of property could easily lead to an assault on all boundaries of privilege and social order, as envisioned by radical religious sects in Britain\'s seventeenth-century civil wars.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Davis underscored the British dilemma: "Britain, when confronted by the rebellious American colonists, hoped to exploit their fear of <a href="/wiki/Slave_rebellion" title="Slave rebellion">slave revolts</a> while also reassuring the large number of slave-holding Loyalists and wealthy Caribbean planters and merchants that their slave property would be secure".<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The Colonists, however, accused the British of encouraging slave revolts.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>American advocates of independence were commonly lampooned in Britain for what was termed their hypocritical calls for freedom, at the same time that many of their leaders were planters who held hundreds of slaves. <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a> snapped, "how is it we hear the loudest yelps for liberty among the [slave] drivers of the Negroes?"<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Benjamin Franklin countered by criticizing the British self-congratulation about "the freeing of one Negro" (<a href="/wiki/Somersett%27s_Case" class="mw-redirect" title="Somersett&#39;s Case">Somersett</a>) while they continued to permit the <a href="/wiki/Slave_Trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave Trade">Slave Trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Phyllis_Wheatley" class="mw-redirect" title="Phyllis Wheatley">Phyllis Wheatley</a> was a black poet who popularized the image of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(name)" title="Columbia (name)">Columbia</a> to represent America. She came to public attention when her <i>Poems on Various Subjects, Religious and Moral</i> appeared in 1773.<sup id="cite_ref-Hochschild_p.50-51_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hochschild_p.50-51-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the war, slaves escaped from New England and the mid-Atlantic area to British-occupied cities such as New York. The effects of the war were more dramatic in the South. In Virginia, royal governor Lord <a href="/wiki/John_Murray,_4th_Earl_of_Dunmore" title="John Murray, 4th Earl of Dunmore">Dunmore</a> recruited black men into the British forces with the promise of freedom, protection for their families, and land grants. Tens of thousands of slaves escaped to British lines throughout the South, causing dramatic losses to slaveholders and disrupting cultivation and harvesting of crops. For instance, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> was estimated to have lost about 25,000 slaves to flight, migration, or death\xe2\x80\x94amounting to one third of its slave population. From 1770 to 1790, the black proportion of the population (mostly slaves) in South Carolina dropped from 60.5 percent to 43.8 percent, and from 45.2 percent to 36.1 percent in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British forces gave transportation to 10,000 slaves when they evacuated <a href="/wiki/Savannah,_Georgia" title="Savannah, Georgia">Savannah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston</a>, carrying through on their promise.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> They evacuated and resettled more than 3,000 <a href="/wiki/Black_Loyalist" title="Black Loyalist">Black Loyalists</a> from New York to <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Upper_Canada" title="Upper Canada">Upper Canada</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lower_Canada" title="Lower Canada">Lower Canada</a>. Others sailed with the British to England or were resettled as freedmen in the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a> of the Caribbean. But slaves who were carried to the Caribbean under control of Loyalist masters generally remained slaves until British abolition in its colonies in 1834. More than 1,200 of the Black Loyalists of Nova Scotia later resettled in the British colony of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sierra_Leone" title="History of Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>, where they became leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Creole_people" title="Sierra Leone Creole people">Krio</a> ethnic group of Freetown and the later national government. Many of their descendants still live in Sierra Leone, as well as other African countries.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_the_Revolution">Effects of the Revolution</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loyalist_expatriation">Loyalist expatriation</span></h3>\n<p>Tens of thousands of Loyalists left the United States&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 the current high estimate is 70,000 by Maya Jasanoff.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>  Some migrated to Britain. The great majority, known as <a href="/wiki/United_Empire_Loyalists" class="mw-redirect" title="United Empire Loyalists">United Empire Loyalists</a>, received land and subsidies for resettlement in British colonies in North America, especially <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Quebec_(1763-1791)" class="mw-redirect" title="Province of Quebec (1763-1791)">Quebec</a> (concentrating in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Townships" title="Eastern Townships">Eastern Townships</a>), <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">Prince Edward Island</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The new colonies of Upper Canada (now <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>) and <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a> were expressly created by Britain for their benefit, where the Crown awarded land to Loyalists as compensation for losses in the United States. Britain wanted to develop the frontier of Upper Canada on a British colonial model. But about 80% of the Loyalists stayed in the United States and became full, loyal citizens; some of the exiles later returned to the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretations">Interpretations</span></h3>\n<p>Interpretations vary concerning the effect of the Revolution. Contemporaries of the period referred to it as "the revolution",<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> although the war is sometimes known as the "American War of Independence" outside the United States, particularly in the United Kingdom.\n</p><p>Historians such as <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Bailyn" title="Bernard Bailyn">Bernard Bailyn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gordon_S._Wood" title="Gordon S. Wood">Gordon Wood</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Morgan_(historian)" title="Edmund Morgan (historian)">Edmund Morgan</a> view the American Revolution as a unique and radical event that produced deep changes and had a profound effect on world affairs, such as an increasing belief in the principles of the Enlightenment. These were demonstrated by a leadership and government that espoused protection of natural rights, and a system of laws chosen by the people.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> John Murrin, by contrast, argues that the definition of "the people" at that time was mostly restricted to free men who were able to pass a property-qualification.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-U.S._Voting_Rights_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Voting_Rights-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> This view argues that any significant gain of the revolution was irrelevant in the short term to women, black Americans and slaves, poor white men, youth, and American Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Morgan has argued that, in terms of long-term impact on American society and values:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>The Revolution did revolutionize social relations. It did displace the deference, the patronage, the social divisions that had determined the way people viewed one another for centuries and still view one another in much of the world. It did give to ordinary people a pride and power, not to say an arrogance, that have continued to shock visitors from less favored lands. It may have left standing a host of inequalities that have troubled us ever since. But it generated the egalitarian view of human society that makes them troubling and makes our world so different from the one in which the revolutionists had grown up.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inspiring_all_colonies">Inspiring all colonies</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Revolutions" title="Atlantic Revolutions">Atlantic Revolutions</a></div>\n<p>After the Revolution, genuinely democratic politics became possible in the former colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> The rights of the people were incorporated into state constitutions. Concepts of liberty, individual rights, equality among men and hostility toward corruption became incorporated as core values of liberal republicanism. The greatest challenge to the old order in Europe was the challenge to inherited political power and the democratic idea that government rests on the <a href="/wiki/Consent_of_the_governed" title="Consent of the governed">consent of the governed</a>. The example of the first successful revolution against a European empire, and the first successful establishment of a republican form of democratically elected government, provided a model for many other colonial peoples who realized that they too could break away and become self-governing nations with directly elected representative government.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Dutch Republic, also at war with Britain, was the next country to sign a treaty with the United States, on October 8, 1782.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamilton,_1974_p._28_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton,_1974_p._28-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> On April 3, 1783, Ambassador Extraordinary <a href="/wiki/Gustaf_Philip_Creutz" title="Gustaf Philip Creutz">Gustaf Philip Creutz</a>, representing King <a href="/wiki/Gustav_III_of_Sweden" title="Gustav III of Sweden">Gustav III of Sweden</a>, and Benjamin Franklin, signed a <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Amity_and_Commerce_(USA%E2%80%93Sweden)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Amity and Commerce (USA\xe2\x80\x93Sweden)">Treaty of Amity and Commerce</a> with the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamilton,_1974_p._28_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton,_1974_p._28-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The American Revolution was the first wave of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Revolutions" title="Atlantic Revolutions">Atlantic Revolutions</a>: the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">Haitian Revolution</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_wars_of_independence" title="Latin American wars of independence">Latin American wars of independence</a>. Aftershocks reached Ireland in the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1798" title="Irish Rebellion of 1798">Irish Rebellion of 1798</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish\xe2\x80\x93Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish\xe2\x80\x93Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>, and in the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Revolution had a strong, immediate influence in Great Britain, Ireland, the Netherlands, and France. Many British and Irish <a href="/wiki/British_Whig_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="British Whig Party">Whigs</a> spoke in favor of the American cause. In Ireland, there was a profound impact; the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Ascendancy" title="Protestant Ascendancy">Protestants who controlled Ireland</a> were demanding more and more self-rule. Under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Grattan" title="Henry Grattan">Henry Grattan</a>, the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Irish_Patriot_Party#Grattan&#39;s_Patriots" title="Irish Patriot Party">Patriots</a>" forced the reversal of mercantilist prohibitions against trade with other British colonies. The King and his cabinet in London could not risk another rebellion on the American model, and made a series of concessions to the Patriot faction in Dublin. Armed Protestant volunteer units were set up to protect against an invasion from France. As in America, so too in Ireland the King no longer had a monopoly of lethal force.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Revolution, along with the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Revolt" title="Dutch Revolt">Dutch Revolt</a> (end of the 16th century) and the 17th century <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>, was among the examples of overthrowing an old regime for many Europeans who later were active during the era of the French Revolution, such as the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Marquis de Lafayette</a>. The American Declaration of Independence influenced the French <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_the_Citizen" class="mw-redirect" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and the Citizen</a> of 1789.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> The spirit of the Declaration of Independence led to laws ending slavery in all the Northern states and the Northwest Territory, with New Jersey the last in 1804. States such as New Jersey and New York adopted gradual emancipation, which kept some people as slaves for more than two decades longer.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_of_American_women">Status of American women</span></h3>\n<p>The democratic ideals of the Revolution inspired changes in the roles of women.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The concept of <a href="/wiki/Republican_motherhood" title="Republican motherhood">republican motherhood</a> was inspired by this period and reflects the importance of <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_republicanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolutionary republicanism">Republicanism as the dominant American ideology</a>. It assumed that a successful republic rested upon the virtue of its citizens. Women were considered to have the essential role of instilling their children with values conducive to a healthy republic. During this period, the wife\'s relationship with her husband also became more liberal, as love and affection instead of obedience and subservience began to characterize the ideal marital relationship. In addition, many women contributed to the war effort through fundraising and running family businesses in the absence of husbands.\n</p><p>The traditional constraints gave way to more liberal conditions for women. Patriarchy faded as an ideal; young people had more freedom to choose their spouses and more often used birth control to regulate the size of their families. Society emphasized the role of mothers in child rearing, especially the patriotic goal of raising republican children rather than those locked into aristocratic value systems. There was more permissiveness in child-rearing. Patriot women married to Loyalists who left the state could get a divorce and obtain control of the ex-husband\'s property.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>\nWhatever gains they had made, however, women still found themselves subordinated, legally and socially, to their husbands, disfranchised and usually with only the role of mother open to them. But, some women earned livelihoods as midwives and in other roles in the community, which were not originally recognized as significant by men.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail Adams</a> expressed to her husband, the president, the desire of women to have a place in the new republic:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I desire you would remember the Ladies, and be more generous and favourable to them than your ancestors. Do not put such unlimited power into the hands of the Husbands.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The Revolution sparked a discussion on the rights of woman and an environment favorable to women\'s participation in politics. Briefly the possibilities for women\'s rights were highly favorable, but a backlash led to a greater rigidity that excluded women from politics.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For more than thirty years, however, the 1776 <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_State_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="New Jersey State Constitution">New Jersey State Constitution</a> gave the vote to "all inhabitants" who had a certain level of wealth, including unmarried women and blacks (not married women because they could not own property separately from their husbands), until in 1807, when that state legislature passed a bill interpreting the constitution to mean universal <i>white male</i> <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">suffrage</a>, excluding paupers.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_of_African_Americans">Status of African Americans</span></h3>\n<p>In the first two decades after the American Revolution, state legislatures and individuals took actions to free numerous slaves, in part based on revolutionary ideals. Northern states passed new constitutions that contained language about equal rights or specifically abolished slavery; some states, such as New York and New Jersey, where slavery was more widespread, passed laws by the end of the 18th century to abolish slavery by a gradual method; in New York, the last slaves were freed in 1827.\n</p><p>While no southern state abolished slavery, for a period individual owners could free their slaves by personal decision, often providing for manumission in wills but sometimes filing deeds or court papers to free individuals. Numerous slaveholders who freed their slaves cited revolutionary ideals in their documents; others freed slaves as a reward for service. Records also suggest that some slaveholders were freeing their own <a href="/wiki/Mixed-race" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed-race">mixed-race</a> children, born into slavery to slave mothers.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemorations">Commemorations</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Commemoration_of_the_American_Revolution" title="Commemoration of the American Revolution">Commemoration of the American Revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial" title="United States Bicentennial">United States Bicentennial</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_revolution_bicentennial.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/American_revolution_bicentennial.svg/250px-American_revolution_bicentennial.svg.png" width="250" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/American_revolution_bicentennial.svg/375px-American_revolution_bicentennial.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/American_revolution_bicentennial.svg/500px-American_revolution_bicentennial.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1042" data-file-height="1042" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_revolution_bicentennial.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial" title="United States Bicentennial">United States Bicentennial</a>, 1976</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/220px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/330px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/440px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1520" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Governor\'s Palace, <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Williamsburg" title="Colonial Williamsburg">Colonial Williamsburg</a>, a restored colonial city in Williamburg, Virginia</div></div></div>\n<p>The American Revolution has a central place in the American memory<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> as the story of the nation\'s founding. It is covered in the schools, memorialized by a <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">national holiday</a>, and commemorated in innumerable monuments. George Washington\'s estate at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vernon" title="Mount Vernon">Mount Vernon</a> was one of the first national pilgrimages for tourists and attracted 10,000 visitors a year by the 1850s.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Revolution became a matter of contention in the 1850s in the debates leading to the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> (1861\xe2\x80\x9365), as spokesmen of both the <a href="/wiki/Northern_United_States" title="Northern United States">Northern United States</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a> claimed that their region was the true custodian of the legacy of 1776.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial" title="United States Bicentennial">United States Bicentennial</a> in 1976 came a year after the American withdrawal from the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, and speakers stressed the themes of renewal and rebirth based on a restoration of traditional values.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Today, more than 100 <a href="/wiki/Category:American_Revolutionary_War_sites" title="Category:American Revolutionary War sites">battlefields and historic sites of the American Revolution</a> are protected and maintained by the government.  The <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a> alone owns and maintains more than 50 battlefield parks and sites related to the Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Battlefield_Trust" title="American Battlefield Trust">American Battlefield Trust</a> preserves almost 700 acres of battlefield land in six states.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bibliography_of_the_American_Revolutionary_War" Revolution</a></li></ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div
